You are a document structure analyst specializing in educational textbooks.

## Task
Analyze the FULL TEXT of an educational document and identify ALL structural chapter/section headings.
Each line is a text block: [ID|pageNumber|optionalTypeTag] text

## CRITICAL: Table of Contents vs. Document Body
The first few pages may contain a TABLE OF CONTENTS (listing chapter names with page numbers).
You MUST IGNORE the actual Table of Contents lists.
HOWEVER, do NOT ignore "Front Matter" sections (like Introduction, Preface) or "Unit" title pages that appear before the main chapters.

Table of contents signs (IGNORE these):
- Pages explicitly labeled "Contents" or "Table of Contents"
- Lists of titles followed by page numbers (e.g., "1.1 ... 5", "Chapter 2 ... 12")
- Dense lists of headings on a single page

## What ARE Structural Headings (INCLUDE)
- **Front Matter**: Introduction, Preface, How to use this book, Acknowledgements, etc.
- **Back Matter**: Glossary, Index, Answers, etc.
- **Organization Containers**: Units, Modules, Parts, Themes (e.g., "Unit 1", "Module A")
- **Chapters**: (e.g., "Chapter 1", "1 Review of number concepts")
- **Sections**: (e.g., "1.1 Different types of numbers")
- **Sub-sections**: (e.g., "1.1.1", "Case Study")
- **Assessments**: "Past paper questions", "Project", "Review", "Summary"

## What Are NOT Headings (MUST EXCLUDE)
- The actual Table of Contents list itself
- Instructional labels: "Key points", "Tip", "Note", "Activity", "Exercise", "Q&A"
- Problem type labels: "Question 1", "Section A", "Multiple Choice"
- Running headers/footers (often repeated at top/bottom of pages)

## Hierarchy (Map to logical levels)
        - **Level 1**: Top-level containers.
          - Front Matter / Back Matter categories (e.g., "Front Matter", "Back Matter")
          - Units / Parts / Modules / Themes (e.g., "Unit 1", "Part A")
          - Standalone Chapters if no Units exist
        - **Level 2**: Main content divisions.
          - Chapters (if inside a Unit)
          - Sections (if inside a standalone Chapter)
          - Specific Front/Back Matter sections (e.g., "Introduction", "Foreword", "Glossary")
        - **Level 3**: Sub-divisions.
          - Sections (if inside a Unit->Chapter)
          - Sub-sections
          - Specific items like "1.1 Understanding units"
        - **Level 4**: Deep sub-divisions (if needed).
          - Sub-sections (e.g., "1.1.1")

        ## Fragmented Titles
If a heading is split across consecutive blocks (e.g., block 100="Chapter" + block 101="1"), merge them: output id as [100, 101].

## Completeness
- Include EVERY structural heading from the very beginning (Introduction) to the very end (Index).
- Do not skip "Unit" pages.

## Output Format
Return ONLY valid JSON:
{
  "directory": [
    {"id": <number or [n1, n2]>, "level": <1|2|3>, "title": "<heading text>"},
    ...
  ]
}
Array MUST be in the LOGICAL order of the document.

[301|p13|T1] Chapter 1
[302|p13|T1] Review of number concepts
[303|p13|T1] IN THIS CHAPTER YOU WILL:
[304|p13] - identify and classify different types of numbers
[305|p13] find common factors and common multiples of numbers
[306|p13] write numbers as products of their prime factors
[307|p13] work with integers used in real-life situations
[308|p13] calculate with powers and roots of numbers
[309|p13] understand the meaning of indices
[310|p13] use the rules of indices
[311|p13] - revise the basic rules for operating with numbers
[312|p13] perform basic calculations using mental methods and with a calculator
[313|p13] round numbers in different ways to estimate and approximate answers.
[314|p14|T1] GETTING STARTED
[315|p14] 1 A lot of the work in this chapter is revision. Look through the chapter to see what is covered.
[316|p14] a Are there any parts of this chapter that you could confidently skip? Explain why.
[317|p14] b If you only had to do three topics in this chapter, which would you choose? Why?
[318|p14] 2 Look at this completed cross-number puzzle.
[319|p14] a Write a set of clues for the puzzle. Each clue should include at least one of the concepts from this chapter.
[320|p14] b Find the sum of the three greatest numbers. Write the answer in words.
[321|p14] 3 Write each of the following using only numbers and brackets if needed.
[322|p14] a nine cubed
[323|p14] b twelve squared
[324|p14] c seven to the power of five
[325|p14] d the reciprocal of three to the power of two
[326|p14] e the reciprocal of three-quarters to the power of zero.
[327|p14] f nine to the power of half
[328|p14] g fourteen billion, ten thousand and nineteen
[329|p14] 4 Look at this decision diagram for problems involving calculation.
[330|p14] [Image]
[331|p14] a Give an example of a problem where an approximate answer is good enough.
[332|p14] b How do you decide which method to use when an exact answer is needed?
[333|p14] Estimates are useful for all of the methods in this decision tree. How could you convince someone that it is important to estimate even if you can use a calculator?

[355|p15|T1] 1.1 Different types of numbers
[356|p15] Make sure you know the correct mathematical words for the types of numbers in the table.
[357|p15] [Table Row] <tr><td>Number</td><td>Definition</td><td>Example</td></tr>
[358|p15] [Table Row] <tr><td>Natural number</td><td>Any whole number from 1 to infinity, sometimes called ‘counting numbers’. 0 is not included.</td><td>1, 2, 3, 4, 5, ...</td></tr>
[359|p15] [Table Row] <tr><td>Odd number</td><td>A whole number that cannot be divided exactly by 2.</td><td>1, 3, 5, 7, ...</td></tr>
[360|p15] [Table Row] <tr><td>Even number</td><td>A whole number that can be divided exactly by 2.</td><td>2, 4, 6, 8, ...</td></tr>

[620|p21|T1] 1.3 Prime numbers
[621|p21] Prime numbers have exactly two different factors: one and the number itself.
[622|p21] Composite numbers have more than two factors.
[623|p21] The number 1 has only one factor so it is not prime and it is not composite.
[624|p21|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[625|p21] Later in this chapter you will learn how to write integers as products of prime factors. One of the reasons why it is important for 1 to NOT be defined is prime is to make sure that the prime factoris...

[638|p21] Cross out 1, it is not prime.
[639|p21] Circle 2, then cross out other multiples of 2.
[640|p21] Circle 3, then cross out other multiples of 3.
[641|p21] Circle the next available number then cross out all its multiples.
[642|p21] Repeat until all the numbers in the table are either circled or crossed out.
[643|p21] The circled numbers are the primes.

[645|p22] You should try to memorise the prime numbers between 1 and 100.

[647|p22|T1] INVESTIGATION
[648|p22] Why do mathematicians find prime numbers exciting?
[649|p22] One reason why prime numbers are interesting and intriguing is because there is a lot about them that we don't know and that mathematicians have not been able to prove.
[650|p22] 1 Goldbach's conjecture (1742) is one of the oldest and best-known unsolved problems in number theory.
[651|p22] a What is Goldbach's strong conjecture?
[652|p22] b A Peruvian mathematician, Harald Helfgott, has published a largely accepted proof of Goldbach's weak conjecture. Find out more about this.
[653|p22|T1] LINK
[654|p22] Today anyone can join the search for Mersenne prime numbers. This project links thousands of home computers to search continuously for larger and larger prime numbers while the computer processors hav...
[655|p22|T1] LINK
[656|p22] Prime numbers are used in codes and codebreaking. The larger the prime you use, the harder it is to break the code. This is why it is more and more important to find larger and larger primes.

[659|p23] a Riemann built his ideas on the prime number theorem. Find out what this is and express it in simple language.
[660|p23] b Is there a proof for the existence of infinitely-many prime numbers?

[669|p23|T1] Prime factors
[670|p23] Prime factors are the factors of a number that are also prime numbers.
[671|p23] Every composite whole number can be broken down and written as the product of its prime factors. You can do this using tree diagrams or using division. Both methods are shown in Worked example 5.
[672|p23|T1] TIP
[673|p23] Remember, a product is the answer to a multiplication. So to write a number as the product of its prime factors you write it like this:
[674|p23] [Equation]
[675|p24|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 5
[676|p24] Write the following numbers as the product of prime factors.
[677|p24] a 36
[678|p24] b 48
[679|p24|T1] Answers
[680|p24] Using a factor tree
[681|p24] [Image]
[682|p24] [Equation]
[683|p24] [Image]
[684|p24] [Equation]
[685|p24] Write the number as two factors.
[686|p24] If a factor is a prime number, circle it.
[687|p24] If a factor is a composite number, split it into two factors.
[688|p24] Keep splitting until you end up with two primes.
[689|p24|T1] TIP
[690|p24] Prime numbers only have two factors: 1 and the number itself. As 1 is not a prime number, do not include it when expressing a number as a product of prime factors.
[691|p24] Using division
[692|p24] [Equation]
[693|p24] [Equation]
[694|p24] [Equation]
[695|p24] [Equation]
[696|p24] Write the primes in ascending order with [EQ] signs.
[697|p24] Divide by the smallest prime number that will go into the number exactly.
[698|p24] Continue dividing, using the smallest prime number that will go into your new answer each time.
[699|p24] Stop when you reach 1.
[700|p24] Write the prime factors in ascending order with [EQ] signs.
[701|p24|T1] TIP
[702|p24] Choose the method that works best for you and stick to it. Always show your method when using prime factors.
[703|p24|T1] Exercise 1.8
[704|p24] 1 Express the following numbers as the product of prime factors.
[705|p24] a 30
[706|p24] f 504
[707|p24] b 24
[708|p24] g 650
[709|p24] c 100
[710|p24] h 1125
[711|p24] d 225
[712|p24] i 756
[713|p24] e 360
[714|p24] i 9240
[715|p24|T1] TIP
[716|p24] When you write your number as a product of primes, group all occurrences of the same prime number together.
[717|p25|T1] Using prime factors to find the HCF and LCM
[718|p25] When you are working with larger numbers you can determine the HCF or LCM by expressing each number as a product of its prime factors.
[719|p25|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 6
[720|p25] Find the HCF of 168 and 180.
[721|p25|T1] Answer
[722|p25] [Equation]
[723|p25] First express each number as a product of prime factors. Use tree diagrams or division to do this.
[724|p25] [Equation]
[725|p25] Underline the factors common to both numbers.
[726|p25] [Equation]
[727|p25] Multiply these out to find the HCF.
[728|p25] [Equation]
[729|p25|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 7
[730|p25] Find the LCM of 72 and 120.
[731|p25|T1] Answer
[732|p25] [Equation]
[733|p25] First express each number as a product of prime factors. Use tree diagrams or division to do this.
[734|p25] [Equation]
[735|p25] Underline the largest set of multiples of each factor.
[736|p25] [Equation]
[737|p25] List these and multiply them out to find the LCM.
[738|p25] [Equation]

[777|p26|T1] 1.4 Working with directed numbers
[778|p26] When you use numbers to represent real-life situations like temperatures, altitude, depth below sea level, profit or loss and directions (on a grid), you sometimes need to use the negative sign to ind...
[779|p26|T1] TIP
[780|p26] Once a direction is chosen to be positive, the opposite direction is taken to be negative. So:
[781|p26] if up is positive, down is negative
[782|p26] if right is positive, left is negative.
[783|p26] if north is positive, south is negative
[784|p26] if above 0 is positive, below 0 is negative.

[807|p27] - Adding a negative number is the same as subtracting the number. [EQ]
[808|p27] - Subtracting a negative number is the same as adding a positive number. [EQ]
[809|p27] Multiplying or dividing the same signs gives a positive answer. [EQ] and [EQ]
[810|p27] - Multiplying or dividing different signs gives a negative answer. [EQ] and [EQ] .

[1103|p35|T1] The laws of indices
[1104|p35] The laws of indices are a set of mathematical rules that allow you to multiply and divide numbers written in index notation without having to write them in expanded form.
[1105|p35] Make sure that you remember these three important rules.
[1106|p35] To multiply different powers of the same number, add the indices. For example, [EQ] and [EQ] .
[1107|p35] To divide different powers of the same number, subtract the indices.

[1109|p35] To find the power of a power you multiply the indices. For example, [EQ] and [EQ]

[1264|p38|T1] 1.6 Order of operations
[1265|p38] At this level of mathematics you are expected to carry out calculations involving more than one operation [EQ] and [EQ] ). When you do this you have to follow a sequence of rules so that there is no c...
[1266|p38] complete operations in grouping symbols first
[1267|p38] - deal with powers and roots next
[1268|p38] do division and multiplication next, working from left to right.
[1269|p38] do addition and subtraction last, working from left to right.
[1270|p38] Many people use the letters BODMAS to remember the order of operations.
[1271|p38] The letters stand for:
[1272|p38] Brackets
[1273|p38] Orders
[1274|p38] Divide Multiply
[1275|p38] Add Subtract
[1276|p38] BODMAS indicates that indices (powers of) are considered after brackets but before all other operations.
[1277|p38|T1] Grouping symbols
[1278|p38] The most common grouping symbols in mathematics are brackets. Here are some examples of the different kinds of brackets used in mathematics:
[1279|p38] [Equation]
[1280|p38|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[1281|p38] You will apply the order of operation rules to fractions, decimals and algebraic expressions as you progress through the course.
[1282|p39] When you have more than one set of brackets in a calculation, you work out the innermost set first.
[1283|p39] Other symbols used to group operations are:
[1284|p39] - fraction bars, e.g. [EQ]
[1285|p39] - root signs, such as square roots and cube roots, e.g. [EQ]

[1508|p43|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 15
[1509|p43] Round 64.839906 to:
[1510|p43] a the nearest whole number
[1511|p43] b 1 decimal place
[1512|p43] c 3 decimal places.
[1513|p43|T1] Answers
[1514|p43] a 64.839906
[1515|p43] 4 is in the units place.
[1516|p43] 64.839906
[1517|p43] The next digit is 8, so you will round up to get 5.
[1518|p43] [EQ] (to nearest whole number)
[1519|p43] To the nearest whole number.
[1520|p43] b 64.839906
[1521|p43] 8 is in the first decimal place.
[1522|p43] 64.839906
[1523|p43] The next digit is 3, so the 8 will remain unchanged.
[1524|p43] [EQ]
[1525|p43] Correct to 1 decimal place.
[1526|p43] c 64.832906
[1527|p43] 9 is in the third decimal place.
[1528|p43] 64.839906
[1529|p43] The next digit is 9, so you need to round up.
[1530|p43] [EQ]
[1531|p43] When you round 9 up, you get 10, so carry one to the previous digit and write 0 in the place of the 9.
[1532|p43] Correct to 3 decimal places.
[1533|p43] When a number has many digits or decimal places it is useful to round it to significant figures (s.f.). The first significant digit of a number is the first non-zero digit, when reading from left to r...
[1534|p43] If you are rounding to a whole number, write the appropriate number of zeros after the last significant digit as place holders to keep the number the same size.
[1535|p43|T1] TIP
[1536|p43] Rounding to 1 significant figure does not mean you will only have one digit. When 13432 is rounded to 1 significant figure it is 10000 and not 1.
[1537|p43|T1] LINK
[1538|p43] We use 'rounding' in all subjects where numerical data is collected. Masses in physics, temperatures in biology, prices in economics: these all need to be recorded sensibly and will be rounded to a de...

[1679|p47|T1] SUMMARY
[1680|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>Do you know ...?</td></tr>
[1681|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>Numbers can be classified as natural numbers, integers, prime numbers and square numbers.</td></tr>
[1682|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>A multiple is obtained by multiplying a number by a natural number. The LCM of two or more numbers is the lowest multiple found in all the sets of multiples.</td></tr>
[1683|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>A factor of a number divides into it exactly. The HCF of two or more numbers is the highest factor found in all the sets of factors.</td></tr>
[1684|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>Prime numbers have only two factors, 1 and the number itself. The number 1 is not a prime number.</td></tr>
[1685|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>A prime factor is a number that is both a factor and a prime number.</td></tr>
[1686|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>All natural numbers that are not prime can be expressed as a product of prime factors.</td></tr>
[1687|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>Integers are also called directed numbers. The sign of an integer (- or +) indicates whether its value is above or below 0.</td></tr>
[1688|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>When you multiply an integer (a) by itself you get a square number (a2). If you multiply it by itself again you get a cube number (a3).</td></tr>
[1689|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>The number you multiply to get a square is called the square root and the number you multiply to get a cube is called the cube root. The symbol for square root is √. The symbol for...
[1690|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>You can express numbers as powers of their factors using index notation. For example, 23 means 2 × 2 × 2. The base is 2 and the index is 3.</td></tr>
[1691|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>Any number to the power of 0 is equal to 1: a0 = 1.</td></tr>
[1692|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>A negative index can be written as a reciprocal fraction with a positive index: a-m = 1/a-m.</td></tr>
[1693|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>Fractional indices can be rewritten as roots: a1/2 = a/2.</td></tr>
[1694|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>For non-unit fractional indices: a1/2 = (a/2)1/2 = a1/m.</td></tr>
[1695|p47] [Table Row] <tr><td>The laws of indices are: a1/m × a1 = a1/m+n; a1/m = a1/m-n and (a1/m)1 = a1/m.</td></tr>

[1856|p53] [EQ] Twice means two times, so you multiply by two.
[1857|p53] d [EQ] Half means divided by two.

[2376|p65|T1] 2.4 Working with brackets
[2377|p65] When an expression has brackets, you normally have to remove the brackets before you can simplify the expression. Removing the brackets is called expanding the expression.
[2378|p65] To remove brackets you multiply each term inside the bracket by the number (and/ or variables) outside the bracket. When you do this you need to pay attention to the positive and negative signs in fro...
[2379|p65] [Equation]
[2380|p65] [Equation]
[2381|p65] [Equation]
[2382|p65] [Equation]

[2384|p65] Expanding brackets is really just multiplying, so the same rules you used for multiplication apply in these examples.

[2391|p65|T1] Answers
[2392|p65] [Image]
[2393|p65] For parts (a) to (d) write out the expression, or do the multiplication mentally.
[2394|p65] [Image]
[2395|p65] Follow these steps when multiplying by a term outside a bracket:
[2396|p65] - Multiply the term on the left-hand inside of the bracket first - shown by the red arrow labelled i.
[2397|p65] - Then multiply the term on the right-hand side - shown by the blue arrow labelled ii.
[2398|p65] Then add the answers together.

[2586|p69|T1] 2.5 Indices
[2587|p69|T1] Revisiting index notation
[2588|p69] When you write a number using indices (powers) you have written it in index notation. Any number can be used as an index including 0, negative integers and fractions.
[2589|p69] The index tells you how many times the base has been multiplied by itself. So:
[2590|p69] [Table Row] <tr><td>\( 3 \times 3 \times 3 \times 3 = {3}^{4} \)</td><td>3 is the base, 4 is the index</td></tr>
[2591|p69] [Table Row] <tr><td>\( a \times a \times a \times a \times a = {a}^{5} \)</td><td>\( a \) is the base, 5 is the index</td></tr>
[2592|p69|T1] TIP
[2593|p69] Try not to carry out too many steps at once. Show every term of your expansion and then simplify.

[2965|p78] Complete your own [EQ] summary to assess your learning in this chapter.
[2966|p78|T1] SUMMARY
[2967|p78|T1] Do you know ...?
[2968|p78] Algebra has special conventions (rules) that allow you to write mathematical information in short ways.
[2969|p78] Letters in algebra are called variables, the number before a letter is called a coefficient and numbers on their own are called constants.
[2970|p78] A group of numbers and variables is called a term. Terms are separated by [EQ] and [EQ] signs, but not by [EQ] or [EQ] signs.
[2971|p78] Like terms have exactly the same combination of variables and powers. You can add and subtract like terms. You can multiply and divide like and unlike terms.
[2972|p78] The order of operations rules for numbers (BODMAS) apply in algebra as well.
[2973|p78] Removing brackets (multiplying out) is called expanding the expression. Collecting like terms is called simplifying the expression.
[2974|p78] Powers are also called indices. The index tells you how many times a number or variable is multiplied by itself. Indices only apply to the number or variable immediately before them.
[2975|p78] The laws of indices are a set of rules for simplifying expressions with indices. These laws apply to positive, negative, zero and fractional indices.
[2976|p79|T1] SUMMARY CONTINUED
[2977|p79] Are you able to ...?
[2978|p79] use letters to represent numbers
[2979|p79] write expressions to represent mathematical information
[2980|p79] To substitute letters with numbers to find the value of an expression.
[2981|p79] add and subtract like terms to simplify expressions
[2982|p79] multiply and divide to simplify expressions
[2983|p79] expand expressions by removing brackets and getting rid of other grouping symbols
[2984|p79] use and make sense of positive, negative and zero indices
[2985|p79] We apply the laws of indices to simplify expressions.
[2986|p79] work with fractional indices
[2987|p79] solve exponential equations using fractional indices.

[3161|p86] [Table Row] <tr><td>Obtuse angle</td><td>An obtuse angle is &gt;90° but &lt;180°.</td><td>A P Q B C ABC &gt; 90° PQR &gt; 90°</td></tr>

[3168|p87|T1] Measuring and drawing angles
[3169|p87] The size of an angle is the amount of turn from one arm of the angle to the other. Angle sizes are measured in degrees [EQ] from 0 to 360 using a protractor.
[3170|p87] [Image]
[3171|p87] A [EQ] protractor has two scales. You need to choose the correct one when you measure an angle.
[3172|p87|T1] LINK
[3173|p87] Geographers and other geoscientists use a piece of equipment called a 'clinometer' to measure angles and calculate the heights of cliffs on the coastline. A clinometer looks and behaves quite like a p...
[3174|p87] [Image]
[3175|p87|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[3176|p87] You will use these skills when drawing pie charts in Chapter 4.
[3177|p87|T1] TIP
[3178|p87] If the arm of the angle does not extend up to the scale, extend the arm past the scale. The length of the arms of the angle does not affect the size of the angle.
[3179|p88|T1] Measuring angles [EQ]
[3180|p88] When an angle is smaller than [EQ] you can read the size from the scale onto the protractor.

[3230|p90|T1] Drawing angles
[3231|p90] You can draw an angle of any given size if you have a ruler, a protractor and a sharp pencil. Work through this example to remind yourself how to draw angles [EQ] and [EQ] .
[3232|p90|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 2
[3233|p90] Draw each angle:
[3234|p90] a [EQ]
[3235|p90] b [EQ]
[3236|p90] Answers
[3237|p90] a
[3238|p90] [Image]
[3239|p90] Use a ruler to draw a line to represent one arm of the angle, make sure the line extends beyond the protractor.
[3240|p90] Mark the vertex (B).
[3241|p90] [Image]
[3242|p90] Place your protractor on the line with the centre at the vertex.
[3243|p90] Measure the size of the angle you wish to draw and mark a small point.
[3244|p90] [Image]
[3245|p90] Remove the protractor and use a ruler to draw a line from the vertex through the point.
[3246|p90] Label the angle correctly.
[3247|p90] b
[3248|p90] [Image]
[3249|p90] For a reflex angle, draw a line as in (a) but mark one arm (X) as well as the vertex (Y). The arm should extend beyond the vertex to create a [EQ] angle.

[3274|p91|T1] Angle relationships
[3275|p91] Make sure you know the following angle facts:
[3276|p91] Complementary angles
[3277|p91] Angles in a right angle add up to [EQ] .
[3278|p92] When the sum of two angles is [EQ] those two angles are complementary angles.
[3279|p92] [Image]
[3280|p92] [Image]
[3281|p92|T1] Supplementary angles
[3282|p92] Angles on a straight line add up to [EQ] .
[3283|p92] When the sum of two angles is [EQ] those two angles are supplementary angles.
[3284|p92] [Image]
[3285|p92] [Image]
[3286|p92|T1] Angles round a point
[3287|p92] Angles at a point make a complete revolution.
[3288|p92] The sum of the angles at a point is [EQ]

[3290|p92] [Image]

[3292|p92|T1] Vertically opposite angles
[3293|p92] When two lines intersect, two pairs of vertically opposite angles are formed.
[3294|p92] Vertically opposite angles are equal in size.
[3295|p92] [Image]
[3296|p92] [Image]
[3297|p92] [Image]
[3298|p92|T1] TIP
[3299|p92] In general terms:
[3300|p92] For complementary angles, if one angle is [EQ] , the other must be [EQ] and vice versa.
[3301|p92] For supplementary angles, if one angle is [EQ] , the other must be [EQ] and vice versa.
[3302|p92|T1] TIP
[3303|p92] The adjacent angle pairs in vertically opposite angles form pairs of supplementary angles because they are also angles on a straight line.
[3304|p93|T1] Using angle relationships to find unknown angles
[3305|p93] You can use the relationships between angles to work out the size of unknown angles.
[3306|p93] To do this:
[3307|p93] identify the relationship
[3308|p93] make an equation
[3309|p93] - give reasons for statements
[3310|p93] - solve the equation to find the unknown value.

[3369|p95|T1] Angles and parallel lines
[3370|p95] When two parallel lines are cut by a third line (the transversal) eight angles are formed. These angles form pairs which are related to each other in specific ways.
[3371|p95|T1] Corresponding angles ( [EQ] -shape)
[3372|p95] When two parallel lines are cut by a transversal four pairs of corresponding angles are formed. Corresponding angles are equal to each other.
[3373|p95] [Image]
[3374|p95] [Image]
[3375|p95] [Image]
[3376|p95] [Image]
[3377|p95|T1] Alternate angles (‘Z’-shape)
[3378|p95] When two parallel lines are cut by a transversal two pairs of alternate angles are formed. Alternate angles are equal to each other.
[3379|p95] [Image]
[3380|p95] [Image]
[3381|p95|T1] Co-interior angles ('C'-shape)
[3382|p95] When two parallel lines are cut by a transversal two pairs of co-interior angles are formed. Co-interior angles are supplementary (together they add up to [EQ] ).
[3383|p95] [Image]
[3384|p95] [Image]
[3385|p95|T1] TIP
[3386|p95] Although [EQ] and [EQ] shapes help you to remember these properties, you must use the terms corresponding, 'alternate' and cointerior' to describe them when you answer a question.
[3387|p95|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[3388|p95] You will use the angle relationships in this section again when you deal with triangles, quadrilaterals, polygons and circles later in this chapter.
[3389|p95|T1] TIP
[3390|p95] 'Co-means together. Co-interior angles are found together on the same side of the transversal.
[3391|p95] Co-interior angles will only be equal if the transversal is perpendicular to the parallel lines (when they will both be [EQ] ).
[3392|p96] The angle relationships around parallel lines and the other angle relationships from earlier in the chapter are very useful for solving unknown angles in geometry.

[3434|p98|T1] 3.2 Triangles
[3435|p98] A triangle is a plane shape with three sides and three angles.
[3436|p98] Triangles are classified according to the lengths of their sides and the sizes of their angles (or both).
[3437|p98|T1] LINK
[3438|p98] If you fix three straight sticks or rods together in a triangle it is not possible for the triangle to change its shape without breaking. This is why so many engineering structures use triangles for r...
[3439|p98] [Image]
[3440|p98|T1] TIP
[3441|p98] Plane means flat. Plane shapes are flat or two-dimensional shapes.
[3442|p98|T1] Scalene triangle
[3443|p98] [Image]
[3444|p98] Scalene triangles have no sides of equal length and no angles that are of equal sizes.
[3445|p98|T1] Isosceles triangle
[3446|p98] [Image]
[3447|p98] Isosceles triangles have two sides of equal length. The angles at the base of the equal sides are equal in size.
[3448|p99|T1] Equilateral triangle
[3449|p99] [Image]
[3450|p99] Equilateral triangles have three equal sides and three equal angles (each is [EQ]
[3451|p99|T1] Other triangles
[3452|p99] [Image]
[3453|p99] [Image]
[3454|p99] [Image]
[3455|p99] Acute-angled triangles have all angles [EQ]
[3456|p99] Right-angled triangles have one angle [EQ]
[3457|p99] Obtuse-angled triangles have one angle [EQ]
[3458|p99|T1] Angle properties of triangles
[3459|p99] The three angles inside a triangle are called interior angles.
[3460|p99] If you extend a side of a triangle you make another angle outside the triangle. Angles outside the triangle are called exterior angles.
[3461|p99] Look at the following diagram carefully to see two important angle properties of triangles.
[3462|p99] [Image]
[3463|p99] [Image]
[3464|p99] [Image]
[3465|p99] The diagram shows two things:
[3466|p99] the three interior angles of a triangle add up to [EQ]
[3467|p99] - two interior angles of a triangle are equal to the opposite exterior angle.
[3468|p99] If you try this yourself with any triangle you will get the same results. But why is this so? Mathematicians cannot just show things to be true, they have to prove them using mathematical principles. ...
[3469|p100] Angles in a triangle add up to [EQ]
[3470|p100] To prove this you can draw a line parallel to one side of the triangle.
[3471|p100] [Image]
[3472|p100] [Image]
[3473|p100] [Equation]
[3474|p100] but:
[3475|p100] [Equation]
[3476|p100] [Equation]
[3477|p100] The exterior angle is equal to the sum of the opposite interior angles
[3478|p100] [Image]
[3479|p100] [Equation]
[3480|p100] [Equation]
[3481|p100] [Equation]
[3482|p100] [Equation]
[3483|p100] [Equation]
[3484|p100] [Equation]
[3485|p100] These two properties allow you to find the missing angles in triangles and other diagrams involving triangles.
[3486|p100|T1] TIP
[3487|p100] You don't need to know these proofs, but you do need to remember the rules associated with them.

[3565|p103|T1] 3.3 Quadrilaterals
[3566|p103] Quadrilaterals are plane shapes with four sides and four interior angles.
[3567|p103] Quadrilaterals are named and classified according to their properties.
[3568|p103|T1] TIP
[3569|p103] Some of these shapes are actually 'special cases' of others. For example, a square is also a rectangle because opposite sides are equal and parallel and all angles are [EQ] . Similarly, any rhombus is...
[3570|p103] [Table Row] <tr><td>Type of quadrilateral</td><td>Examples</td><td>Summary of properties</td></tr>
[3571|p103] [Table Row] <tr><td>Parallelogram</td><td>a = c b = d</td><td>Opposite sides parallel and equal. Opposite angles are equal. Diagonals bisect each other.</td></tr>
[3572|p103] [Table Row] <tr><td>Rectangle</td><td></td><td>Opposite sides parallel and equal. All angles = 90°. Diagonals are equal. Diagonals bisect each other.</td></tr>
[3573|p103] [Table Row] <tr><td>Square</td><td></td><td>All sides equal. All angles = 90°. Diagonals equal. Diagonals bisect each other at 90°. Diagonals bisect angles.</td></tr>
[3574|p103] [Table Row] <tr><td>Rhombus</td><td>a = c b = d</td><td>All sides equal in length. Opposite sides parallel. Opposite angles equal. Diagonals bisect each other at 90°. Diagonals bisect angles.</td></tr...
[3575|p103] [Table Row] <tr><td>Trapezium</td><td></td><td>One pair of sides parallel.</td></tr>
[3576|p103] [Table Row] <tr><td>Kite</td><td>a = b c = d</td><td>Two pairs of adjacent sides equal. One pair of opposite angles is equal. Diagonals intersect at 90°.</td></tr>
[3578|p104|T1] TIP
[3579|p104] Each diagonal on a square or rhombus is the perpendicular bisector of the other because they intersect at [EQ] and divide each other into two equal lengths.
[3580|p104|T1] The angle sum of a quadrilateral
[3581|p104] All quadrilaterals can be divided into two triangles by drawing one diagonal. You already know that the angle sum of a triangle is [EQ] . Therefore, the angle sum of a quadrilateral is [EQ] .
[3582|p104] This is an important property and you can use it together with the other properties of quadrilaterals to find the sizes of unknown angles.

[3771|p111|T1] 3.6 Construction
[3772|p111] In geometry, constructions are accurate geometrical drawings. You use mathematical instruments to construct geometrical drawings.

[3849|p115] I know all the key words and their meanings and I use them in my work.

[3856|p115|T1] SUMMARY
[3857|p115|T1] Do you know ...?
[3858|p115] A point is position and a line is the shortest distance between two points.
[3859|p115] Parallel lines are the same distance apart along their length.
[3860|p115] Perpendicular lines meet at right angles.
[3861|p115] Acute angles are [EQ] ,right angles are exactly [EQ] ,obtuse angles are [EQ] but [EQ] . Straight angles are exactly [EQ] . Reflex angles are [EQ] but [EQ] . A complete revolution is [EQ] .
[3862|p115] Scalene triangles have no equal sides, isosceles triangles have two equal sides and a pair of equal angles, and equilateral triangles have three equal sides and three equal angles.
[3863|p115] Complementary angles have a sum of [EQ] . Supplementary angles have a sum of [EQ] .
[3864|p115] Angles on a line have a sum of [EQ]
[3865|p115] Angles round a point have a sum of [EQ]
[3866|p115] Vertically opposite angles are formed when two lines intersect. Vertically opposite angles are equal.

[3868|p116|T1] Do you know ...?
[3869|p116] When a transversal cuts two parallel lines various angle pairs are formed. Corresponding angles are equal. Alternate angles are equal. Co-interior angles are supplementary.
[3870|p116] The angle sum of a triangle is [EQ]
[3871|p116] The exterior angle of a triangle is equal to the sum of the two opposite interior angles.
[3872|p116] Quadrilaterals can be classified as parallelograms, rectangles, squares, rhombuses, trapeziums or kites according to their properties.
[3873|p116] The angle sum of a quadrilateral is [EQ] .
[3874|p116] Polygons are many-sided plane shapes. Polygons are named according to the number of sides they have, e.g. pentagon (5), hexagon (6), octagon (8) and decagon (10).
[3875|p116] Regular polygons have equal sides and equal angles.
[3876|p116] Irregular polygons have unequal sides and unequal angles.
[3877|p116] The angle sum of a polygon is [EQ] , where [EQ] is the number of sides.
[3878|p116] The angle sum of exterior angles of any convex polygon is [EQ]

[3950|p121|T1] 4.1 Collecting and classifying data
[3951|p121] Data is a set of facts, numbers or other information. Statistics involves a process of collecting data and using it to try and answer a question. The flow diagram shows the four main steps involved in...
[3952|p121] [Image]
[3953|p121|T1] Different types of data
[3954|p121] Answer these two questions:
[3955|p121] Who is your favourite singer?
[3956|p121] How many people in your class wear glasses?
[3957|p121] Your answer to the first question will be the name of a person. Your answer to the second question will be a number. Both the name and the number are types of data.
[3958|p121] Categorical data is non-numerical data. It names or describes something without reference to number or size. Colours, names of people and places, yes and no answers, opinions and choices are all categ...
[3959|p121] Numerical data is data in number form. It can be an amount, a measurement, a time or a score. Numerical data is also called quantitative data (from the word quantity).
[3960|p121|T1] TIP
[3961|p121] Data is actually the plural of the Latin word datum, but in modern English the word data is accepted and used as a singular form, so you can talk about a set of data, this data, two items of data or a...
[3962|p121|T1] TIP
[3963|p121] The information that is stored on a computer hard-drive or your phone is also called data. In computer terms, data has nothing to do with statistics, it just means stored information.
[3964|p121|T1] LINK
[3965|p121] All of this work is very important in the fields of human and social sciences, where scientists need to present data to inform their conclusions.
[3966|p122] Numerical data can be further divided into two groups:
[3967|p122] - discrete data - this is data that can only take certain values, for example, the number of students in a class, goals scored in a match or red cars passing a point. When you count things, you are co...
[3968|p122] - continuous data - this is data that can take any value between two given values, for example, the height of a person who is between [EQ] and [EQ] tall could be [EQ] , [EQ] , [EQ] , [EQ] or any other...
[3969|p122|T1] TIP
[3970|p122] One way to decide if data is continuous is to ask whether it is possible for the values to be fractions or decimals. If the answer is yes, the data is usually continuous. But be careful:
[3971|p122] - age may seem to be discrete, because it is often given in full years, but it is actually continuous because we are getting older all the time
[3972|p122] - shoe sizes are discrete, even though you can get shoes in half sizes, because you don't get shoes in size [EQ] or [EQ] or [EQ] .
[3973|p122|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[3974|p122] You will need to fully understand continuous data when you study histograms in Chapter 20.

[3977|p122] Data can also be collected from secondary sources. This involves using existing data to find the information you need. For example, if you use data from an internet site or even from the pages of this...

[4017|p124|T1] 4.2 Organising data
[4018|p124] When you collect a large amount of data you need to organise it so that it is easy to read and use. Tables (tally tables, frequency tables and two-way tables) are the most commonly used methods of org...
[4019|p124|T1] Tally tables
[4020|p124] A tally table is used to keep a record when you count things.
[4021|p124|T1] TIP
[4022|p124] Remember each tally (I) shows one item. Tallies are grouped in fives (JH) to make it easier to get a total when you need one.
[4023|p124|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS

[4153|p129|T1] Stem-and-leaf diagrams
[4154|p129] A stem-and-leaf diagram allows you to organise and display grouped data using the actual data values. When you use a frequency table to organise grouped data you cannot see the actual data values, jus...
[4155|p129|T1] LINK
[4156|p129] Sociologists and geographers sometimes draw population pyramids. These are sideways bar charts that show the numbers of people in various age categories. These charts look similar to stem-and-leaf dia...
[4157|p129] In a stem-and-leaf diagram each data item is broken into two parts: a stem and a leaf. The final digit of each value is the leaf and the previous digits are the stem. The stems are written to the left...
[4158|p129] [Table Row] <tr><td>Stem</td><td>Leaf</td></tr>
[4159|p129] [Table Row] <tr><td>1</td><td>3</td></tr>
[4160|p129] In this case, the tens digit is the stem and the units digit is the leaf.
[4161|p129] A larger data value such as 259 is shown as:
[4162|p129] [Table Row] <tr><td>Stem</td><td>Leaf</td></tr>
[4163|p129] [Table Row] <tr><td>25</td><td>9</td></tr>
[4164|p129] In this case, the stem represents both the tens and the hundreds digits while the units digit is the leaf.
[4165|p129] To be useful, a stem-and-leaf diagram should have at least five stems. If the number of stems is less than that, you can split the leaves into two (or sometimes even five) classes. If you do this, eac...
[4166|p129] [Table Row] <tr><td>Stem</td><td>Leaf</td></tr>
[4167|p129] [Table Row] <tr><td>1</td><td>0 3 4 2 1</td></tr>
[4168|p129] [Table Row] <tr><td>1</td><td>5 9 8 7 5 6</td></tr>
[4169|p129] Values from 10 to 14 (leaves 0 to 4) are included in the first class, values from 15 to 19 (leaves 5 to 9) are included in the second class.
[4170|p129] Stem-and-leaf diagrams are easier to work with if the leaves are ordered from smallest to greatest.
[4171|p129|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[4172|p129] You will work with stem-and-leaf diagrams again when you calculate averages and measures of spread in Chapter 12.
[4173|p130|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 2
[4174|p130] This data set shows the ages of customers in a café one lunchtime.
[4175|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>34</td><td>23</td><td>40</td><td>35</td><td>25</td><td>28</td><td>18</td><td>32</td></tr>
[4176|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>37</td><td>29</td><td>19</td><td>17</td><td>32</td><td>55</td><td>36</td><td>42</td></tr>
[4177|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>33</td><td>20</td><td>25</td><td>34</td><td>48</td><td>39</td><td>36</td><td>30</td></tr>
[4178|p130] Draw a stem-and-leaf diagram to display this data.
[4179|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>Stem</td><td>Leaf</td></tr>
[4180|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>1</td><td>8 9 7</td></tr>
[4181|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>2</td><td>3 5 8 9 0 5</td></tr>
[4182|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>3</td><td>4 5 2 7 6 6 3 4 2 9 0</td></tr>
[4183|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>4</td><td>0 2 8</td></tr>
[4184|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>5</td><td>5</td></tr>
[4185|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>Stem</td><td>Leaf</td><td>Key</td></tr>
[4186|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>1</td><td>7 8 9</td><td rowspan="5">1 | 7 represents 17</td></tr>
[4187|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>2</td><td>0 3 5 5 8 9</td></tr>
[4188|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>3</td><td>0 2 2 3 4 4 5 6 6 7 9</td></tr>
[4189|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>4</td><td>0 2 8</td></tr>
[4190|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>5</td><td>5</td></tr>
[4191|p130] Group the ages in intervals of ten, 10-19; 20-29 and so on.
[4192|p130] These are two-digit numbers, so the tens digit is the stem.
[4193|p130] List the stems in ascending order down the left of the diagram.
[4194|p130] Work through the data in the order it is given, writing the units digits (the leaves) in a row next to the appropriate stem. Space the leaves to make them easier to read.
[4195|p130] If you need to work with the data, you can redraw the diagram, putting the leaves in ascending order.
[4196|p130] From this reorganised stem-and-leaf diagram you can quickly see that:
[4197|p130] the youngest person using the internet cafe was 17 years old (the first data item)
[4198|p130] the oldest person was 55 (the last data item)
[4199|p130] - most users were in the age group 30-39 (the group with the largest number of leaves).
[4200|p130] If you want to show two sets of data, you can use a back-to-back stem-and-leaf diagram. The second set of data is plotted against the same stem, but the leaves are written to the left.
[4201|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>Brand X Leaf</td><td>Stem</td><td>Brand Y Leaf</td></tr>
[4202|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>9 4 8 7 2</td><td>0</td><td>5 8</td></tr>
[4203|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>7 8 7 2 3</td><td>1</td><td>4 7 8 2</td></tr>
[4204|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>8 4 6 2 7 9 8</td><td>2</td><td>8 9 7 1 5</td></tr>
[4205|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>7 2</td><td>3</td><td>7 2 1 0</td></tr>
[4206|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td></td><td>4</td><td>2</td></tr>
[4207|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td></td><td>5</td><td>1</td></tr>
[4208|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>Key</td></tr>
[4209|p130] [Table Row] <tr><td>Brand X 8 | 2 represents 28 hours Brand Y 4 | 2 represents 42 hours</td></tr>
[4210|p130] This stem-and-leaf diagram compares the battery life (between charges) of two different brands of mobile phone. You read the data for Brand X from right to left. The stem is still the tens digit.

[4247|p132|T1] Two-way tables
[4248|p132] A two-way table shows the frequency of certain results for two or more sets of data. Here is a two-way table showing how many people in cars were wearing their seat belts when they passed a check poin...
[4249|p132] [Table Row] <tr><td></td><td>Wearing a seat belt</td><td>Not wearing a seat belt</td></tr>
[4250|p132] [Table Row] <tr><td>Drivers</td><td>10</td><td>4</td></tr>
[4251|p132] [Table Row] <tr><td>Passengers</td><td>6</td><td>3</td></tr>
[4252|p132] The headings at the top of the table give you information about wearing seat belts. The headings down the side of the table give you information about drivers and passengers.
[4253|p133] You can use the table to find out:
[4254|p133] how many drivers were wearing seat belts
[4255|p133] how many passengers were wearing seat belts
[4256|p133] how many drivers were not wearing seat belts
[4257|p133] how many passengers were not wearing seat belts.
[4258|p133] You can also add the totals across and down to work out:
[4259|p133] how many drivers were surveyed
[4260|p133] how many passengers were surveyed
[4261|p133] - how many people (drivers and passengers) were wearing seat belts or not wearing seat belts.
[4262|p133] Here are two more examples of two-way tables:
[4263|p133] Drinks and crisps sold at a school snack shop during lunch break
[4264|p133] [Table Row] <tr><td></td><td>Sweet chilli</td><td>Plain</td><td>Cheese and onion</td></tr>

[4299|p134|T1] Two-way tables in everyday life

[4312|p135|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 3 CONTINUED
[4313|p135] a What was the total population of the world in mid-2021?
[4314|p135] b By how much is the population of the world expected to grow by 2035?
[4315|p135] c What percentage of the world's population lived in Asia in mid-2021? Give your answer to the closest whole per cent.
[4316|p135] d i Which region is likely to experience a decrease in population between 2021 and 2035?
[4317|p135] ii What is the population of this region likely to be in 2035?
[4318|p135] iii By how much is the population of this region expected to decrease by 2050?
[4319|p135|T1] Answers
[4320|p135] a 7837000000
[4321|p135] Read this from the table.
[4322|p135] b 8848000000-7837000000=101100000
[4323|p135] Read the value for 2035 from the table and subtract the smaller figure from the larger.
[4324|p135] 4651000000 7837000000 × 100 = 59.3467% ≈ 59%
[4325|p135] Read the figures from the table and then calculate the percentage.
[4326|p135] d i Europe
[4327|p135] Look to see which numbers are decreasing across the row.
[4328|p135] ii 742000000
[4329|p135] Read this from the table.
[4330|p135] iii 744000000-731000000=13000000
[4331|p135] Read the values from the table and subtract the smaller figure from the larger.

[4353|p136|T1] 4.3 Using charts to display data
[4354|p136] Charts are useful for displaying data because you can see patterns and trends easily and quickly. You can also compare different sets of data easily. In this section you are going to revise what you a...
[4355|p136|T1] Pictograms
[4356|p136] Pictograms are fairly simple charts. Small symbols (pictures) are used to represent quantities. The meaning of the symbol and the amount it represents (a 'key') must be provided for the graph to make ...
[4357|p136|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 4
[4358|p136] This pictogram shows the amount of time that five friends spent talking on their phones during one week.
[4359|p136] [Image]

[4361|p136] a Who spent the most time on the phone that week?
[4362|p136] b How much time did Isobel spend on the phone that week?
[4363|p136] c Who spent [EQ] hours on the phone that week?
[4364|p136] d Draw the symbols you would use to show [EQ] hours.
[4365|p137|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 4 CONTINUED
[4366|p137|T1] Answers
[4367|p137] a Anna
[4368|p137] The person with the most clocks.
[4369|p137] b 3 hours
[4370|p137] There are three whole clocks; the key shows you that each one stands for 1 hour. The fourth clock is only three-quarters, so it must be [EQ] of an hour.
[4371|p137] c Tara
[4372|p137] She has three full clocks, each worth 1 hour, and one half clock.
[4373|p137] d
[4374|p137] [Image]
[4375|p137] [Image]
[4376|p137] [Image]
[4377|p137] Two full clocks to represent two hours, and a quarter of a clock to represent [EQ] hours.

[4408|p138|T1] Bar charts
[4409|p138] Bar charts are normally used to display discrete data. The chart shows information as a series of bars plotted against a scale on the axis. The bars can be horizontal or vertical.
[4410|p138] [Image]
[4411|p139] [Image]
[4412|p139] There are different methods of drawing bar charts, but all bar charts should have:
[4413|p139] a title that tells you what data is being displayed
[4414|p139] - a number scale or axis (so you can work out how many are in each class) and a label on the scale that tells you what the numbers stand for
[4415|p139] a scale or axis that lists the categories displayed
[4416|p139] - bars that are equally wide and equally spaced
[4417|p139] - bars that do not touch for qualitative of discrete data.
[4418|p139|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 5

[4429|p140|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[4430|p140] A bar chart is not the same as a histogram. A histogram is often used for continuous data. You will learn more about histograms in Chapter 20.

[4437|p141|T1] Composite bar charts
[4438|p141] A composite (stacked) bar chart breaks the whole set of data shown on each bar into groups.
[4439|p141] This composite bar graph shows the percentage of the total time that different athletes spend on three events in a triathlon. The bars are the same length because they all represent [EQ] of the time t...

[4443|p141] [Image]
[4444|p141|T1] TIP
[4445|p141] Each colour section represents a share of the whole. You have to work out what that share is by counting along the scale from the start of each colour.

[4481|p143|T1] PEER ASSESSMENT
[4482|p143] How can you decide whether another student's bar chart is correct or not?
[4483|p143] One way of assessing a piece of work is to write a checklist. A checklist is an assessment tool. It is a list of statements that give the criteria you will use to measure skills or student success in ...
[4484|p143] 1 Work in small groups to write a checklist to assess whether someone can draw an accurate and clear bar chart.
[4485|p143] Consider the learning outcomes for this work and what you have covered in the lessons.
[4486|p143] - Your statements should be clear, specific and easy to check. For example: The chart has a clear heading [ ] yes [ ] no
[4487|p143] Leave space in your checklist to add comments. For example: Your heading has spelling mistakes in it. or The vertical scale needs to start at 0.
[4488|p143] 2 Use your checklist to assess another student's completed work in Exercise 4.8.
[4489|p143] 3 Use the checklist to suggest corrections, improvements or learning goals that still need to be met.

[4497|p144] - Which activities helped you to learn the most? Why?
[4498|p144] Did you experience any difficulty or frustration in this work? What did you do about it?

[4519|p145] The table shows how a student spent one day.
[4520|p145] [Table Row] <tr><td>Activity</td><td>School</td><td>Sleeping</td><td>Eating</td><td>Online</td><td>On the phone</td><td>Complaining about stuff</td></tr>
[4521|p145] [Table Row] <tr><td>Number of hours</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>1.5</td><td>3</td><td>2.5</td><td>2</td></tr>
[4522|p145] Draw a pie chart to show this data.
[4523|p145] [EQ] First work out the total number of hours.
[4524|p145] Then work out each category as a fraction of the whole and convert the fraction to degrees:
[4525|p145] [Table Row] <tr><td></td><td>(as a fraction of 24)</td><td>(convert to degrees)</td></tr>
[4526|p145] [Table Row] <tr><td>School</td><td>= 7/24</td><td>= 7/24 × 360 = 105°</td></tr>
[4527|p145] [Table Row] <tr><td>Sleeping</td><td>= 8/24</td><td>8/24 × 360 = 120°</td></tr>
[4528|p145] [Table Row] <tr><td>Eating</td><td>= 1.5/24 = 15/240</td><td>15/240 × 360 = 22.5°</td></tr>
[4529|p145] [Table Row] <tr><td>Online</td><td>= 3/24</td><td>3/24 × 360 = 45°</td></tr>
[4530|p145] [Table Row] <tr><td>On the phone</td><td>= 2.5/24 = 25/240</td><td>25/240 × 360 = 37.5°</td></tr>
[4531|p145] [Table Row] <tr><td>Complaining about stuff</td><td>= 2/24</td><td>2/24 × 360 = 30°</td></tr>
[4532|p145] [Table Row] <tr><td>Activity</td><td>School</td><td>Sleeping</td><td>Eating</td><td>Online</td><td>On the phone</td><td>Complaining about stuff</td></tr>
[4533|p145] [Table Row] <tr><td>Number of hours</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>1.5</td><td>3</td><td>2.5</td><td>2</td></tr>
[4534|p145] [Table Row] <tr><td>Angle</td><td>105°</td><td>120°</td><td>22.5°</td><td>45°</td><td>37.5°</td><td>30°</td></tr>
[4535|p145] Draw a circle to represent the whole day.
[4536|p145] Use a ruler and a protractor to measure each sector.
[4537|p145] Label the chart and give it a title.
[4538|p145] [Image]
[4539|p145|T1] TIP
[4540|p145] It is possible that your angles, once rounded, don't quite add up to [EQ] . If this happens, you can add or subtract a degree to or from the largest sector (the one with the highest frequency).

[4552|p146] Measure the angle, convert it to hours.
[4553|p146] c Things Javi didn't bother to list. Possibly eating, showering, getting dressed.

[4621|p152] [Table Row] <tr><td>In statistics, data is a set of information collected to answer a particular question.</td></tr>
[4622|p152] [Table Row] <tr><td>Categorical (qualitative) data is non-numerical. Colours, names, places and other descriptive terms are all categorical.</td></tr>
[4623|p152] [Table Row] <tr><td>Numerical (quantitative) data is collected in the form of numbers. Numerical data can be discrete or continuous. Discrete data takes certain values; continuous data can take any va...
[4624|p152] [Table Row] <tr><td>Primary data is data you collect yourself from a primary source. Secondary data is data you collect from other sources (previously collected by someone else).</td></tr>
[4625|p152] [Table Row] <tr><td>Unsorted data is called raw data. Raw data can be organised using tally tables, frequency tables, stem-and-leaf diagrams and two-way tables to make it easier to work with.</td></tr...
[4626|p152] [Table Row] <tr><td>Data in tables can be displayed as graphs to show patterns and trends at a glance.</td></tr>
[4627|p152] [Table Row] <tr><td>Pictograms are simple graphs that use symbols to represent quantities.</td></tr>
[4628|p152] [Table Row] <tr><td>Bar charts have rows of horizontal bars or columns of vertical bars of different lengths. The bar length (or height) represents an amount. The actual amount can be read from a scal...
[4629|p152] [Table Row] <tr><td>Dual bar charts display two or more sets of data side by side on the same set of axes.</td></tr>
[4630|p152] [Table Row] <tr><td>Composite bar charts display groups within a set of data stacked on a single bar.</td></tr>
[4631|p152] [Table Row] <tr><td>Pie charts are circular charts divided into sectors to show categories of data.</td></tr>
[4632|p152] [Table Row] <tr><td>Line graphs use lines to plot changes in the data over time.</td></tr>
[4633|p152] [Table Row] <tr><td>The type of graph you draw depends on the data and what you wish to show.</td></tr>

[4821|p160|T1] Fractions, percentages and standard form
[4822|p160|T1] IN THIS CHAPTER YOU WILL:
[4823|p160] find equivalent fractions

[4834|p161] Sea nettle jellyfish bodies on the previous page are [EQ] protein, muscles and nerve cells - the rest is water. Work out what percentage of a jellyfish's body is water.
[4835|p161] We use fractions and percentages on an almost daily basis, often without realising it. For example, we use fractions to answer questions like these:
[4836|p161] How far can you travel on half a tank of petrol?
[4837|p161] If you share [EQ] of a cake between two people, how much will they each get?
[4838|p161] If three-fifths of a [EQ] journey is complete, how far do you still have to travel?
[4839|p161] If a shop is offering a 25% discount, what will you pay for an item costing $120?

[4858|p162|T1] 5.1 Revisiting fractions
[4859|p162] A fraction tells you what share or part of a whole amount you are working with. For example, [EQ] of a pie means 1 of 2 equal parts of the pie and [EQ] of the students means 7 out of every 8 students....
[4860|p162] Proper fractions are written in the form [EQ] where [EQ] . The number on the top, [EQ] , can be any number and is called the numerator. The number on the bottom, [EQ] , can be any number except 0 and ...
[4861|p162] When you multiply or divide both the numerator and the denominator by the same number you get a new fraction that has the same value as the original fraction. The new fraction is called an equivalent ...
[4862|p162] For example, [EQ] and [EQ] .
[4863|p162] Notice in the second example that the original fraction [EQ] was divided rather than multiplied to get [EQ] . The numbers 5 and 7 have no common factor other than 1 so the fraction cannot be divided a...

[4866|p162|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 1
[4867|p162] Express each fraction in its simplest form.
[4868|p162] a 3 15
[4869|p162] b 16 24
[4870|p162] 21 28
[4871|p162] 5 d 8
[4872|p162|T1] Answers
[4873|p162] 3 3÷3 15 15+3 5
[4874|p162] 16 16+8 2 b 24 24+8 3
[4875|p162] 21+7 3 28+7 4
[4876|p162] d [EQ] is already in its simplest form
[4877|p162] (5 and 8 have no common factors other than 1).
[4878|p162|T1] TIP
[4879|p162] Notice that in each case you divide the numerator and the denominator by their HCF.
[4880|p162|T1] TIP
[4881|p162] Fractions were originally written with a slant line like this: 2/5. You will often find fractions written like this and when you enter fractions online you type them like that.
[4882|p162|T1] TIP
[4883|p162] Remember, dividing the numerator and denominator by the same number simplifies the fraction.

[4925|p164|T1] 5.2 Operations on fractions
[4926|p164|T1] Multiplying fractions
[4927|p164] To multiply two or more fractions, multiply the numerators and then multiply the denominators. Sometimes you will need to simplify your answer.
[4928|p164|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 3
[4929|p164] Calculate.
[4930|p164] a [EQ]
[4931|p164] b [EQ]
[4932|p164] 3 of 4 1 8
[4933|p164] Answers
[4934|p164] a [EQ]
[4935|p164] Another method is to cancel before multiplying:
[4936|p164] [Equation]
[4937|p164] b [EQ]
[4938|p164] 3 of 4 1 3 3 2 16
[4939|p164] Multiply the numerators to get the new numerator value. Do the same with the denominators. Then express the fraction in its simplest form.
[4940|p164] Divide the denominator of the first fraction, and the numerator of the second fraction, by two.
[4941|p164] 15 and 7 do not have a common factor other than 1 and so cannot be simplified.
[4942|p164] You need to change the mixed number to an improper fraction. This allows you to complete the multiplication. Notice that the word 'of' is replaced with the [EQ] sign.
[4943|p164|T1] TIP
[4944|p164] To multiply a fraction by an integer you only multiply the numerator by the integer.
[4945|p164] For example,
[4946|p164] [Equation]
[4947|p164|T1] TIP
[4948|p164] An improper fraction is a fraction where the numerator is larger than the denominator. To change a mixed number to a common fraction, multiply the whole number part (in this case 4) by the denominator...

[4965|p165|T1] Adding and subtracting fractions
[4966|p165] You can add or subtract fractions that have same denominators (common denominator). You can use equivalent fractions to make fractions with the same denominator.
[4967|p165] This example shows how to use the lowest common multiple (LCM) of both denominators to find a common denominator.
[4968|p165|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 4
[4969|p165] Write each of the following as a single fraction in its simplest form.
[4970|p165] a 3 +5 4 6
[4971|p165] b 23-15
[4972|p165|T1] Answers
[4973|p165] a 3 5 4 6
[4974|p165] [Equation]
[4975|p165] b 23-15 11-12 4 7
[4976|p165] [Equation]
[4977|p165] Find the common denominator.
[4978|p165] The LCM of 4 and 6 is 12. Use this as the common denominator and find the equivalent fractions.
[4979|p165] Add the numerators.
[4980|p165] Change the improper fraction to a mixed number.
[4981|p165] Change mixed numbers to improper fractions to make them easier to work with.
[4982|p165] The LCM of 4 and 7 is 28. Use this as the common denominator and find the equivalent fractions.
[4983|p165] Subtract one numerator from the other.
[4984|p165] Change the improper fraction to a mixed number.
[4985|p165|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[4986|p165] You will use the LCM in this section.
[4987|p165] You learnt about this in Chapter 1.
[4988|p165|T1] TIP
[4989|p165] Once you have a common denominator, you only add the numerators. Never add the denominators.
[4990|p165|T1] TIP
[4991|p165] Adding two fractions can result in an improper fraction.
[4992|p165] To write the fraction in its simplest form, rewrite it as a mixed number.

[5039|p167|T1] Dividing fractions
[5040|p167] To divide one fraction by another, you multiply the first fraction by the reciprocal of the second fraction.
[5041|p167] To find the reciprocal of a fraction you invert it. So, the reciprocal of [EQ] is [EQ] and the reciprocal of [EQ] is [EQ] .
[5042|p167] The reciprocal of [EQ] is [EQ] or just 2 and the reciprocal of 5 is [EQ] .

[5087|p168|T1] Fractions with decimals
[5088|p168] In some calculations either the numerator or the denominator, or both, might not be whole numbers. To express these fractions in their simplest forms:
[5089|p168] - find an equivalent fraction where both the numerator and denominator are integers
[5090|p168] - check that the equivalent fraction is in its simplest form.
[5091|p168|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 6
[5092|p168] Simplify each of the following fractions.
[5093|p168] a 0.1 3
[5094|p168] b [EQ]
[5095|p168] 36 0.12
[5096|p168|T1] Answers
[5097|p168] a 0.1 0.1×10 3 3×10
[5098|p168] [Equation]
[5099|p168] Multiply 0.1 by 10 to convert 0.1 to an integer.
[5100|p168] Make sure the fraction is equivalent by also multiplying the denominator (3) by 10.
[5101|p168|T1] TIP
[5102|p168] To divide a fraction by an integer you can either multiply the denominator by the integer, or divide the numerator by the same integer.
[5103|p169|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 6 CONTINUED
[5104|p169] b [EQ]
[5105|p169] [Equation]
[5106|p169] Multiply both the numerator and denominator by 10 to get integers.
[5107|p169] 13 and 24 do not have a HCF other than 1, so you cannot simplify the fraction.
[5108|p169] 36 36×100 0.12 0.12×100
[5109|p169] [Equation]
[5110|p169] Multiply 0.12 by 100 to produce an integer.
[5111|p169] Remember to multiply the numerator by 100, so the fraction is equivalent.
[5112|p169] Simplify the final fraction by cancelling.

[5155|p171] Statistics in the media are often given as percentages to make them easier to understand. For example, a country may report that [EQ] of adults have been fully vaccinated against a disease. However, u...
[5156|p171|T1] Equivalent percentages, fractions and decimals
[5157|p171] Percentages, fractions and decimals are all ways of representing part of a whole.
[5158|p171] To convert a percentage into a decimal, divide by 100. So, [EQ] and [EQ] . (Notice that the digits move two places to the right.)
[5159|p171] To convert a decimal to a percentage, multiply by 100. So, [EQ] and [EQ] . (Notice that the digits move two places to the left.)
[5160|p171] To convert a percentage to a fraction, write the fraction with a denominator of 100 and simplify.

[5247|p173|T1] Finding one number as a percentage of another
[5248|p173] You can express one number as a percentage of another number. To do this, write the first number as a fraction of the second number then multiply by 100.

[5428|p180] [Equation]
[5429|p180] Divide $320 by 80 to find 1%
[5430|p180] Multiply $4 by 100 to find 100%

[5466|p181|T1] 5.4 Standard form
[5467|p181] [Image]
[5468|p181] There are an estimated 10000000000000000000000000 stars in the universe.
[5469|p181] The star Betelgeuse has a mass of 218800000000000000000000000000000 kg.
[5470|p181] A single carbon atom has a mass of 0.000000000000000000000000000000199kg.
[5471|p181] It is clumsy and impractical to write out very large and very small numbers like this. You might make mistakes and add or miss out zeros in calculations. We use standard form to express very small and...
[5472|p181|T1] LINK
[5473|p181] Very small and very large numbers occur often in chemistry; the carbon atom mass, given here, is just one example. The reason this happens a lot is because the subject usually deals with very small ob...
[5474|p181|T1] TIP
[5475|p181] Standard form is also called scientific notation or exponential notation.
[5476|p182|T1] Writing numbers in standard form
[5477|p182] You can write any number in standard form by writing it as a number between 1 and 10 multiplied by a suitable power of 10. In algebraic language we say that any number can be written in the form [EQ] ...
[5478|p182] The numbers at the start of this section can be written in standard form as [EQ] , [EQ] and [EQ] .
[5479|p182] When a number is in standard form, the index (power of 10) tells you how many places the digits have moved and whether they have moved to the right or left.
[5480|p182|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 19
[5481|p182] Write 320000 in standard form.
[5482|p182|T1] Answer
[5483|p182] 3.2
[5484|p182] [Image]
[5485|p182] Write the number with the decimal point after the first non-zero digit to get a number between 1 and 10.
[5486|p182] Work out how many places the first digit needs to move to get back to its original place. In this case, the 3 needs to move five places to the left to get back to its original hundred thousands positi...
[5487|p182] [EQ]
[5488|p182] Work out the index (power of 10). In this case, the original number is bigger than 3.2 so the index will be positive.
[5489|p182] In other words, to move the digit 3 back to its original position you would need to multiply by 105.
[5490|p182|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 20
[5491|p182] 1 Write each of the following numbers in standard form.
[5492|p182] a 0.004
[5493|p182] b 0.00000034
[5494|p182|T1] Answers
[5495|p182] a
[5496|p182] [Image]
[5497|p182] 0.004
[5498|p182] 4.0
[5499|p182] [EQ]
[5500|p182] Start with a number between 1 and 10, in this case 4.
[5501|p182] Compare the position of the first digit: '4' needs to move three places to the right to get from the new number to the original number. In Worked example 19 you saw that moving five places to the left...
[5502|p182] Notice also that the first non-zero digit in 0.004 is in the 3rd place after the decimal point and that the power of 10 is -3.
[5503|p182] b
[5504|p182] 0.00000034=3.4÷107
[5505|p182] [EQ]
[5506|p182] [Image]
[5507|p182] 0000000034=3.4×10-7
[5508|p182] The first non-zero digit in 0.00000034 is in the 7th place after the decimal point so the power of 10 is -7.

[5703|p188|T1] SUMMARY
[5704|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>Do you know ...?</td></tr>
[5705|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>An equivalent fraction can be found by multiplying or dividing the numerator and denominator by the same number.</td></tr>
[5706|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>Fractions can be added or subtracted, but you must make sure that you have a common denominator first.</td></tr>
[5707|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>To multiply two fractions you multiply their numerators and multiply their denominators.</td></tr>
[5708|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>To divide by a fraction you find its reciprocal and then multiply.</td></tr>
[5709|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>Percentages are fractions with a denominator of 100.</td></tr>
[5710|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>Percentage increases and decreases are always percentages of the original value.</td></tr>
[5711|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>You can use reverse percentages to find the original value.</td></tr>
[5712|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>Standard form can be used to write very large or very small numbers quickly.</td></tr>
[5713|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>Are you able to ...?</td></tr>
[5714|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>find a fraction of a number</td></tr>
[5715|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>find a percentage of a number</td></tr>
[5716|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>find one number as a percentage of another number</td></tr>
[5717|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>calculate a percentage increase or decrease</td></tr>
[5718|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>find a value before a percentage change</td></tr>
[5719|p188] [Table Row] <tr><td>do calculations with numbers written in standard form.</td></tr>
[5720|p189|T1] Practice questions

[6099|p200] [Equation]

[6101|p200] For example, to make [EQ] the subject of this formula:
[6102|p200] [EQ] Write down the starting formula.
[6103|p200] [EQ] Subtract [EQ] from both sides (to isolate the term containing [EQ]
[6104|p200] [EQ] Divide both sides by [EQ] (notice that everything on the left is divided by [EQ] ).
[6105|p200] You now have [EQ] on its own and so now [EQ] is the subject of the formula.
[6106|p200] Usually we rewrite this so the subject is on the left:
[6107|p200] [Equation]
[6108|p200] Notice how similar this process is to solving equations.

[6486|p218|T1] Arcs and sectors
[6487|p218] The diagram shows a circle with two radii (plural of radius) drawn from the centre.
[6488|p218] The region contained in-between the two radii is known as a sector. Notice that there is a major sector and a minor sector.
[6489|p218] A section of the circumference is known as an arc.
[6490|p218] The Greek letter [EQ] represents the angle subtended at the centre. Notice that the minor sector is a fraction of the full circle. It is [EQ] of the circle.
[6491|p218] The area of a circle is [EQ] . The sector is [EQ] of the circle, so we can replace 'of' with '×' to give:
[6492|p218] Sector area = θ/360 × πr²
[6493|p218] [Image]
[6494|p219] The circumference of a circle is [EQ] . The sector is [EQ] of the circle, so the length of the arc of the sector is [EQ] of the circumference. So:
[6495|p219] Arc length = 360 × 2πr
[6496|p219] Make sure that you remember these two special cases:
[6497|p219] If [EQ] then you have a quarter of a circle.
[6498|p219] If [EQ] then you have a half of a circle. This is known as a semicircle.

[6572|p223|T1] 7.2 Three-dimensional objects
[6573|p223] The focus of the rest of this chapter is three-dimensional objects, but you will use many of the formulae for two-dimensional shapes in your calculations. A three-dimensional object is called a solid.
[6574|p223|T1] Nets of solids
[6575|p223] A net is a two-dimensional shape that can be drawn, cut out and folded to form a three-dimensional solid.
[6576|p223] This is the net of a solid that you should be quite familiar with.
[6577|p223] [Image]
[6578|p223] If you fold along the dotted lines and join the points with the same letters then you will form this cube.
[6579|p223] You should try this yourself and look carefully at which edges (sides) and which vertices (points or corners) join up.
[6580|p223] [Image]
[6581|p223|T1] TIP
[6582|p223] Cardboard boxes start out as a flat shape. The cardboard is then folded along carefully chosen lines, which become the edges of the box. Try unfolding a box to see how it was made.

[6603|p225|T1] 7.3 Surface areas and volumes of solids
[6604|p225] The flat, two-dimensional surfaces on the outside of a solid are called faces. You can find the area of each face using the techniques from earlier in this chapter. The total area of all the faces is ...
[6605|p225] The volume is the amount of space contained inside the solid. If the lengths are given in centimetres, then the volume is measured in cubic centimetres [EQ] and so on.
[6606|p225|T1] TIP
[6607|p225] We measure the amount of water flowing over a waterfall in cubic metres per second. The maximum flow of water over the Iguazu Falls in South America was 45700 cubic metres per second, on 9 June 2014. ...
[6608|p225|T1] TIP
[6609|p225] It can be helpful to draw the net of a solid when trying to find its surface area.
[6610|p225|T1] Cuboids
[6611|p225] A cuboid has six rectangular faces, twelve edges and eight vertices.
[6612|p225] If the length, width and height of the cuboid are [EQ] and [EQ] (respectively) then you can find the surface area by thinking about the areas of each rectangular face.
[6613|p225] [Image]
[6614|p226] Notice that the surface area is exactly the same as the area of the cuboid's net.
[6615|p226] Surface area of cuboid [EQ]
[6616|p226] [Image]
[6617|p226] The volume of a cuboid is its length [EQ] width [EQ] height.
[6618|p226] Volume of cuboid [EQ]
[6619|p226] [Image]
[6620|p226|T1] Prisms
[6621|p226] A prism is a solid whose cross-section is the same all along its length. (A cross-section is the surface formed when you cut parallel to a face.)
[6622|p226] [Image]
[6623|p226] Prisms are often named according to the shape of their cross-section. For example, a triangular prism has a triangle cross-section, a pentagonal prism has a pentagon cross-section and so on.
[6624|p227] A cuboid is a prism with a rectangular cross-section, so you may sometimes see it called a rectangular prism.
[6625|p227] [Image]
[6626|p227] [Image]
[6627|p227] You can calculate the surface area of a prism by working out the area of each face and adding them together. There are two ends with area equal to the cross-sectional area. The remaining sides are all...
[6628|p227] Surface area of a prism [EQ] area of cross-section + perimeter of cross-section [EQ] length
[6629|p227] You can calculate the volume of a prism by working out the area of the cross-section and multiplying this by the length.
[6630|p227] Volume of a prism [EQ] area of cross-section [EQ] length
[6631|p227|T1] Cylinders
[6632|p227] A cylinder is not a prism, because the cross section has curved edges. The cross section of a cylinder is a circle and is the same all along its length, so a cylinder is a 'tube'.
[6633|p227] [Image]
[6634|p227] [Image]
[6635|p227] A cylinder can be 'unwrapped' to produce its net. The surface of the solid consists of two circular faces and a curved surface that can be flattened to make a rectangle.
[6636|p227] Curved surface area of a cylinder [EQ]
[6637|p227] and
[6638|p227] Volume of a cylinder [EQ]
[6639|p227] [Image]
[6640|p227|T1] LINK
[6641|p227] Deep sea divers carry tanks filled with compressed air on their backs. These tanks are usually shaped like cylinders, making it easy to calculate the volume and to work out how much air is left.
[6642|p227|T1] TIP
[6643|p227] If questions ask for exact answers to surface area and volume calculations where [EQ] is part of the formula, you must give your answer as a multiple of [EQ] .

[6671|p230|T1] Pyramids
[6672|p230] A pyramid is a solid with a polygon-shaped base and triangular faces that meet at a point called the apex.
[6673|p230] You can find the surface area of a pyramid by finding the area of the base and the area of each of the triangles and adding them all together.
[6674|p230] You can find the volume of a pyramid by using the following formula:
[6675|p230] Volume [EQ] base area [EQ] perpendicular height
[6676|p230] The perpendicular height is the shortest distance from the base to the apex.
[6677|p230|T1] Cones
[6678|p230] A cone is a not a pyramid, because it has a circular base and a curved surface. The length [EQ] is known as the slant height and [EQ] is the perpendicular height.
[6679|p230|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[6680|p230] The slant height can be calculated using Pythagoras' theorem, which you will meet in Chapter 11.
[6681|p230] The curved surface of the cone can be opened out and flattened to form a sector of a circle.
[6682|p230] Curved surface area of cone [EQ]
[6683|p230] If you are asked for the total surface area of a cone, you must work out the area of the circular base and add it to the curved surface area.
[6684|p230] [Image]
[6685|p230|T1] TIP
[6686|p230] In general, the perpendicular distance from a point to a line is the shortest distance to the line.
[6687|p230] [Image]
[6688|p231] You can calculate the volume of a cone using the following formula:
[6689|p231] Volume [EQ]
[6690|p231] [Image]

[6696|p231] A hemisphere is half a sphere.

[6758|p235|T1] SUMMARY
[6759|p235|T1] Do you know ...?
[6760|p235] The perimeter is the distance around the outside of a two-dimensional shape and the area is the space contained within the sides.
[6761|p235] Circumference is the name for the perimeter of a circle.
[6762|p235] If the units of length are given in cm then the units of area are cm² and the units of volume are cm³.
[6763|p235] This relationship is also true if the lengths are measured in mm, m, km and so on.
[6764|p235] A sector of a circle is the region contained in-between two radii of a circle. This splits the circle into a minor sector and a major sector.
[6765|p235] An arc is a section of the circumference.
[6766|p235] Prisms, pyramids, spheres, cubes and cuboids are examples of three-dimensional objects (or solids).
[6767|p235] A net is a two-dimensional shape that can be folded to form a solid.
[6768|p235] The net of a solid can be useful when working out the surface area of the solid.
[6769|p235|T1] Are you able to ...?
[6770|p235] To recognise different two-dimensional shapes and find their areas,
[6771|p235] give the units of the area
[6772|p235] calculate the areas of various two-dimensional shapes
[6773|p235] divide a shape into simpler shapes and find the area.
[6774|p235] find unknown lengths when some lengths and an area are given.
[6775|p235] calculate the area and circumference of a circle.
[6776|p235] calculate the perimeter, arc length and area of a sector.
[6777|p235] recognise nets of solids
[6778|p235] find the volumes and surface areas of cuboids, prisms and cylinders.
[6779|p235] find the volumes and surface areas of solids that can be broken into simpler shapes.
[6780|p235] find the volumes and surface areas of pyramids, cones and spheres.

[6824|p240|T1] GETTING STARTED
[6825|p240] Most modern calculators are able to produce random numbers. Usually, there will be a number with the word 'ran' or 'ran#" written on it. For most, if you press this button and then press the = button,...
[6826|p240] 1 Try to work out how to use the random number button on your calculator.
[6827|p240] 2 Play a game of 'higher or lower' with yourself.
[6828|p240] - Start by getting a random number from your calculator.
[6829|p240] - Guess whether the next number will be 'higher' or 'lower'.
[6830|p240] Find another random number and work out whether it was 'higher' or 'lower' than the previous number.
[6831|p240] Were you correct?
[6832|p240] If yes, take another go; if no, stop.
[6833|p240] Write down the number of turns you take without getting it wrong.
[6834|p240] 3 Think about how you can maximise your chances of being correct. For which numbers would you most sensibly choose 'higher'? What about lower?
[6835|p240|T1] 8.1 Understanding basic probability

[6837|p240] Probability is a measure of how likely an event is to happen. Something that is impossible has a value of zero and something that is certain has a value of one. The range of values from zero to one is...
