[6885|p242|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 2
[6886|p242] The numbers one to six are written on six identical cards. The cards are shuffled and placed face down on a table. If a card is selected at random, what is the probability of obtaining:
[6887|p242] a a three
[6888|p242] b an even number
[6889|p242] a prime number?
[6890|p242|T1] Answers
[6891|p242] a P(3) = 1/6
[6892|p242] There is only one way of picking a three, but six possible outcomes (you could select a 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6).
[6893|p242] b P(even number) = 3/6 = 1/2
[6894|p242] There are three ways of picking an even number, giving three favourable outcomes.
[6895|p242] c [EQ]
[6896|p242] The prime numbers you could pick are 2, 3 and 5, giving three favourable outcomes.
[6897|p242|T1] TIP
[6898|p242] In some countries, theoretical probability is referred to as 'expected probability'. This is a casual reference and does not mean the same thing as mathematical 'expectation'.
[6899|p242|T1] TIP
[6900|p242] Questions will usually tell you whether an object or experiment is unbiased.
[6901|p243|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 3
[6902|p243] A card is drawn from an ordinary park of 52 cards. What is the probability that the card will be a king?
[6903|p243|T1] Answer
[6904|p243] [Equation]
[6905|p243] Number of possible outcomes is 52.
[6906|p243] Number of favourable outcomes is four, because there are four kings per pack.
[6907|p243|T1] TIP
[6908|p243] A set of 52 playing cards four suits: hearts, diamonds, clubs and spades. Hearts and diamonds are red. Clubs and spades are black. Each suit is made up of numbers 2 to 10 and an ace, jack, queen and k...
[6909|p243|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 4
[6910|p243] Nasir has 20 socks in a drawer.
[6911|p243] Eight socks are red, ten socks are blue and two socks are green. If a sock is drawn at random, what is the probability that it is green?
[6912|p243|T1] Answer
[6913|p243] P(green) = 2/20 = 1/10
[6914|p243] Number of possible outcomes is 20.
[6915|p243] Number of favourable outcomes is two.
[6916|p243] [Image]
[6917|p243|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 5
[6918|p243] Nine teams of painters were each given one letter from the word HOLLYWOOD to paint. Each painting team was given a letter at random. Find the probability that a team of painters was asked to paint:
[6919|p243] a the letter Y
[6920|p243] b the letter 'O'
[6921|p243] c the letter 'H' or the letter 'L'
[6922|p243] d the letter 'Z'.
[6923|p243|T1] Answers
[6924|p243] a [EQ]
[6925|p243] Number of favourable outcomes is one (there is only one 'Y').
[6926|p243] There are nine possible outcomes in total.
[6927|p243] b [EQ]
[6928|p243] Number of favourable outcomes is three.
[6929|p243] c [EQ] or [EQ]
[6930|p243] Number of favourable outcomes [EQ] number of letters that are either [EQ] or [EQ] since there is one [EQ] and two L's.
[6931|p243] d [EQ]
[6932|p243] Number of favourable outcomes is zero (there are no 'Z's).

[7017|p247|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 7
[7018|p247] One red dice and one blue dice are thrown at the same time and the numbers showing on the dice are added together. Find the probability that:
[7019|p247] a the sum is 7
[7020|p247] b the sum is less than 5.
[7021|p247] c the sum is greater than or equal to 8.
[7022|p247] d the sum is less than 8.
[7023|p247|T1] Answers
[7024|p247] Red
[7025|p247] [Table Row] <tr><td>+</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td></tr>
[7026|p247] [Table Row] <tr><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td></tr>
[7027|p247] [Table Row] <tr><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td></tr>
[7028|p247] [Table Row] <tr><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td></tr>
[7029|p247] [Table Row] <tr><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td></tr>
[7030|p247] [Table Row] <tr><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td></tr>
[7031|p247] [Table Row] <tr><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td></tr>
[7032|p247] The diagram shows that there are 36 possible sums, so there are 36 equally likely outcomes in total.
[7033|p247] a P(7) = 6 1 36 6
[7034|p247] There are six 7s in the grid, so six favourable outcomes.
[7035|p247] b [EQ]
[7036|p247] The outcomes that are less than 5 are 2, 3 and 4. There are six favourable outcomes for these numbers.
[7037|p247] c [EQ]
[7038|p247] The outcomes greater than or equal to 8 (which includes 8) are 8, 9, 10, 11 or 12.
[7039|p247] [Equation]
[7040|p247] These give 15 favourable outcomes.
[7041|p247] d [EQ]
[7042|p247] P(less than 8) = P(not greater than or equal to 8)
[7043|p247] [EQ] (greater than or equal to 8)

[8662|p252] It is not possible to pass and fail an exam at the same time. This is why the events are mutually exclusive.
[8663|p252|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 9
[8664|p252] Simone and Rami are playing basketball. The probability that Simone shoots the ball into the net is 0.1. The probability that Rami shoots the ball into the net is 0.2. Simone's success or failure at s...
[8665|p252] Simone and Rami take one shot each. Find the probability that:
[8666|p252] a both shoot the ball into the net
[8667|p252] b Simone shoots the ball into the net, but Rami does not
[8668|p252] c exactly one ball is shot into the net.
[8669|p252|T1] Answers
[8670|p252] a [EQ] (both shoot the ball into the net) [EQ]
[8671|p252] b [EQ] (Simone shoots the ball into the net but Rami does not) [EQ]
[8672|p252] c [EQ] (exactly one ball is shot into the net) [EQ] (Simone shoots the ball into the net and Rami does not or Simone does not shoot the ball into the net and Rami does)
[8673|p252] [Equation]
[8674|p252] [Image]

[8676|p253] With a partner, discuss some examples of mutually exclusive events. Try to think of real-life examples and not just numbers on a die or the faces of a coin.

[8715|p254|T1] SUMMARY
[8716|p254|T1] Do you know ...?
[8717|p254] Probability measures how likely something is to happen.
[8718|p254] An outcome is the single result of an experiment.
[8719|p254] An event is a collection of favourable outcomes.
[8720|p254] Experimental probability can be calculated by dividing the number of favourable outcomes by the number of trials.
[8721|p254] Favourable outcomes are any outcomes that mean your event has happened.
[8722|p254] If outcomes are equally likely then theoretical probability can be calculated by dividing the number of favourable outcomes by the number of possible outcomes.
[8723|p254] The probability of an event happening and the probability of that event not happening will always sum up to one. If [EQ] is an event, then [EQ] is the event that [EQ] does not happen and [EQ] .
[8724|p254] Independent events do not affect one another.
[8725|p254] Mutually exclusive events cannot happen together.
[8726|p254|T1] Are you able to ...?
[8727|p254] find an experimental probability given the results of several trials
[8728|p254] find a theoretical probability
[8729|p254] find the probability that an event will not happen if you know the probability that it will happen.
[8730|p254] draw a sample space diagram
[8731|p254] recognise independent and mutually exclusive events
[8732|p254] do calculations involving combined probabilities.

[8986|p266|T1] 9.1 Sequences
[8987|p266] A sequence is set of numbers (or a pattern) that follow a particular order. Each number in a sequence is called a term. Terms that follow each other in a sequence are called consecutive terms.
[8988|p266] The position of a term is given using [EQ] so [EQ] is the first term, [EQ] is the fifth term and [EQ] is any term.
[8989|p266] The terms in a sequence usually follow a rule. You can use the rule for a sequence to work out which term comes next or the value of any term in the sequence.
[8990|p266] There are different kinds of sequences.
[8991|p266] Arithmetic sequences have a common (constant) difference between the terms. For example, the sequence 2, 6, 10, 14, ... has a common difference of +4 and the sequence 100, 92, 84, 76, ... has a common...
[8992|p266] Non-linear sequences do not have a common difference between the terms.
[8993|p266] Geometric sequences involve multiplying or dividing by a common ratio. For example, each term in the sequence 3, 6, 12, 24, ... is found by multiplying the previous term by 2 and the sequence 800, 400...
[8994|p266] Some sequences involve powers:
[8995|p266] quadratic sequences involve square numbers. For example, 1, 4, 9, 16, ...
[8996|p266] cubic sequences involve cube numbers. For example, 1, 8, 27, 64, ...
[8997|p266|T1] The term-to-term rule
[8998|p266] Here are some sequences with the rule that tells you how to keep the sequence going:
[8999|p266] 2, 8, 14, 20, 26, 32, ... (get the next term by adding six to the previous term).
[9000|p266] You can show this pattern by drawing it in this way:
[9001|p266] [Image]
[9002|p266] 1, [EQ] (divide each term by two to get the next term).
[9003|p266] This diagram shows how the sequence progresses:
[9004|p266] [Image]
[9005|p266] 1, 2, 4, 8, 16, 32, ... (multiply each term by two to get the next term).
[9006|p266] In diagram form:
[9007|p266] [Image]
[9008|p266|T1] LINK
[9009|p266] Sequences of numbers are often used in cryptography, the science of codebreaking. In a simple code, the letters of the alphabet (which already form a clear sequence) can be matched to letters or numbe...
[9010|p266|T1] LINK
[9011|p266] Chemists often need to understand how quantities change over time. An understanding of sequences can help chemists to understand how a reaction works and this allows them to predict what their results...
[9012|p267] The rule that tells you how to generate the next term in a sequence is called the term-to-term rule. These rules are usually given in words and they tell you which operation to use to work out the nex...

[9027|p267|T1] INVESTIGATION
[9028|p267] 3 The first term [EQ] of a sequence is 5. The term-to-term rule for the sequence is 'add [EQ] '.
[9029|p267] Find the value of [EQ] that will give you each of these sequences:
[9030|p267] a every second term is not an integer
[9031|p267] b every second term is a multiple of 10.
[9032|p267] c [EQ]
[9033|p267|T1] Relating a term to its position in the sequence
[9034|p267] The term-to-term rule is useful for finding the next few terms in a sequence. However, if you are asked to find the 20th term or the 100th term it is not efficient to list all the terms. If you can wo...
[9035|p267] To find the rule you have to work systematically with the term number and the value of each term. Tables are very useful for doing this.
[9036|p267] This table shows the term number and the value of the first five terms in a sequence.
[9037|p267] [Table Row] <tr><td>Term number (n)</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td></tr>

[9507|p284|T1] 9.3 Surds
[9508|p284] Some number have roots that can be written as rational numbers. For example, [EQ] , [EQ] and [EQ] .
[9509|p284] Roots that cannot be written as rational numbers are called surds. [EQ] and [EQ] are all surds. For any root, if [EQ] is an irrational number, the root is a surd.
[9510|p284] A surd is an exact value. If you use a calculator to find [EQ] you get an approximate value of 1.414213562 ... When you are asked for an exact value, you should leave your answer in surd form.
[9511|p284|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[9512|p284] Surds are used to calculate exact values in trigonometry and you will re-use the skills you learn here in Chapter 15. Surds may also be useful in physics when you deal with resultant forces and veloci...

[9524|p285] The general rules that you already know for working with roots apply to surds as well.
[9525|p285] [Table Row] <tr><td>General rule</td><td>Examples using numbers</td></tr>
[9526|p285] [Table Row] <tr><td>(√x)2=x</td><td>(√4)2=√4×√4=2×2=4 (√11)2=√11×√11=11</td></tr>
[9527|p285] [Table Row] <tr><td>√x2=x</td><td>√62=√36=6</td></tr>
[9528|p285] [Table Row] <tr><td>√x×√y=√xy</td><td>√4×√16=2×4=8 This is the same as: √4×√16=√4×16=√64=8</td></tr>
[9529|p285] [Table Row] <tr><td>√x÷√y=√x/y</td><td>√64÷√4=8÷2=4 √64+√4=√64/4=√16=4</td></tr>
[9530|p285] You can apply these rules to simplify and manipulate surds so that you can solve problems involving exact values.
[9531|p285|T1] Simplifying surds
[9532|p285] You can simplify a surd if the number under the root sign has factors that are square numbers.
[9533|p285] To simplify a surd, use the fact that [EQ] to write the surd as a product of smaller roots, one of which is a perfect square.

[9912|p296|T1] 9.4 Sets
[9913|p296] A set is a list or collection of objects that share one or more characteristics. The objects in a set can be anything from numbers, letters and shapes to names, places or paintings, but there is usual...
[9914|p296] The list of members or elements of a set is placed inside a pair of curly brackets [EQ] .
[9915|p296] Some examples of sets are:
[9916|p296] {2,4,6,8,10} - the set of all even integers greater than zero but less than 11
[9917|p296] [EQ] - the set of vowels
[9918|p296] {Red, Green, Blue} - the set containing the colours red, green and blue.
[9919|p296] We use capital letters to name sets:
[9920|p296] If [EQ] is the set of prime numbers less than 10, then: [EQ] .
[9921|p296] If [EQ] is the set of letters in the word 'HAPPY', then: [EQ] .
[9922|p296] Two sets are equal if they contain exactly the same elements, even if the order is different, so:
[9923|p296] [EQ] and so on.
[9924|p296] The number of elements in a set is written as [EQ] , where [EQ] is the name of the set. For example, in the set [EQ] there are five elements so [EQ] .
[9925|p296] A set that contains no elements is known as the empty set. The symbol [EQ] is used to represent the empty set.
[9926|p296] For example:
[9927|p296] {odd numbers that are multiples of two} = [EQ] because no odd number is a multiple of two.
[9928|p296] Now, if [EQ] is a member (an element) of the set [EQ] then it is written: [EQ] .
[9929|p296] If [EQ] is not a member of the set [EQ] , then it is written: [EQ] .
[9930|p296] For example, if [EQ] , then:
[9931|p296] Spades [EQ] but Turtles [EQ]
[9932|p296] Some sets have a number of elements that can be counted. These are known as finite sets. If there is no limit to the number of members of a set then the set is infinite.
[9933|p296] If [EQ] , then [EQ] has 26 members and is finite.
[9934|p296] If [EQ] , then [EQ] and is infinite.

[9936|p296] - sets are listed inside curly brackets { }

[10205|p306|T1] SUMMARY
[10206|p306|T1] Do you know...?
[10207|p306] A sequence is the elements of a set arranged in a particular order, connected by a rule.
[10208|p306] A term is any number in a sequence.
[10209|p306] If the position of a term in a sequence is given the letter [EQ] then a rule can be found to work out the value of the [EQ] th term.
[10210|p306] A rational number is a number that can be written as a fraction.
[10211|p306] An irrational number cannot be written as a fraction and will have a decimal part that continues forever without repeating.
[10212|p306] Sunds are exact values of irrational numbers written with a root sign.
[10213|p306] A surd is in simplest form when it has no factors that are square numbers and when the denominator of a fraction with surds is a rational number.
[10214|p306] A set is a list or collection of objects that share a characteristic.
[10215|p306] An element is a member of a set.
[10216|p306] A set that contains no elements is called the empty set (2).
[10217|p306] A universal set [EQ] contains all the possible elements appropriate to a particular problem.
[10218|p306] The complement of a set is the elements that are not in the set [EQ]
[10219|p306] The elements of two sets can be combined (without repeats) to form the union of the two sets [EQ] .
[10220|p306] The elements that two sets have in common is called the intersection of the two sets [EQ] .
[10221|p306] A Venn diagram is a pictorial method of showing sets.
[10222|p306] A shorthand way of describing the elements of a set is called set builder notation.

[10392|p314] To draw a graph from its equation:

[10395|p314] - plot each point on the number plane
[10396|p314] - draw a straight line to join the points (use a ruler).

[10443|p316] The gradient of a vertical line is undefined because the graph does not move right or left.
[10444|p316] The gradient of a horizontal line is zero (it does not move up or down when you move to the right).

[10774|p328|T1] Finding the length of a straight line segment
[10775|p328] Although lines are infinitely long, you are usually only interested in a part of a line. Any section of a line joining two points is called a line segment.
[10776|p328|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[10777|p328] The word 'segment' means 'part'. You have seen this word used in Chapter 3, where a segment of a circle is the part of a circle 'cut off' by a chord.
[10778|p328] If you know the coordinates of the end points of a line segment you can use Pythagoras' theorem to calculate the length of the line segment.

[11284|p342|T1] SUMMARY
[11285|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>Do you know ...?</td></tr>
[11286|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>The equation of a line tells you how the x- and y-coordinates are related for all points that sit on the line.</td></tr>
[11287|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>The gradient of a line is a measure of its steepness.</td></tr>
[11288|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>The x- and y-intercepts are where the line crosses the x- and y-axes respectively.</td></tr>
[11289|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>The value of m in y=mx+c is the gradient of the line.</td></tr>
[11290|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>The value of c in y=mx+c is the y-intercept.</td></tr>
[11291|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>The x-intercept can be found by substituting y=0 and solving for x.</td></tr>
[11292|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>The y-intercept can be found by substituting x=0 and solving for y.</td></tr>
[11293|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>Two lines with the same gradient are parallel.</td></tr>
[11294|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>The gradients of two perpendicular lines multiply to give -1.</td></tr>
[11295|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>Two brackets can be expanded by multiplying each term in the first bracket by each term in the second bracket.</td></tr>
[11296|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>Some quadratic expressions can be factorised to solve quadratic equations.</td></tr>
[11297|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>Sometimes the two solutions of a quadratic equation might be equal.</td></tr>
[11298|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>Are you able to ...?</td></tr>
[11299|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>draw a line from its equation by constructing a table and plotting points</td></tr>

[11301|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>calculate the gradient of a line from its graph</td></tr>
[11302|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>find the equation of a line if you know its gradient and y-intercept</td></tr>
[11303|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>find the equation of a vertical or horizontal line</td></tr>
[11304|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>calculate the gradient of a line from the coordinates of two points on the line</td></tr>
[11305|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>find the length of a line segment and the coordinates of its midpoint</td></tr>
[11306|p342] [Table Row] <tr><td>expand double brackets</td></tr>

[11563|p355|T1] 11.2 Understanding similar triangles
[11564|p355] Two mathematically similar objects have exactly the same shape and proportions, but may be different in size.
[11565|p355] When one shape is enlarged to produce the second shape, each part of the original will correspond to a particular part of the new shape. For triangles, corresponding sides join the same angles. The ra...
[11566|p355] All of the following are true for similar triangles:
[11567|p355] [Image]
[11568|p355] [Image]
[11569|p355] Corresponding angles are equal.
[11570|p355] [Image]
[11571|p355] [Image]
[11572|p355] 'Internal' ratios of sides are the same for both triangles. For example:
[11573|p355] [Equation]
[11574|p355] [Image]
[11575|p355] [Image]
[11576|p355] Ratios of corresponding sides are equal:
[11577|p355] [Equation]
[11578|p355] If any of these things are true about two triangles, then all of them will be true for both triangles.

[11906|p371|T1] 11.4 Understanding congruence
[11907|p371] Two shapes are congruent if they are the same shape and size.
[11908|p371|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[11909|p371] You will meet rotations and reflections in Chapter 19. When an object is reflected or rotated its shape and size remain unchanged. The resulting object is congruent to the original.
[11910|p371] If two shapes are congruent then:
[11911|p371] corresponding sides are equal in length.
[11912|p371] corresponding angles are equal
[11913|p371] the shapes have the same area.
[11914|p371] Look at these pairs of congruent shapes. The corresponding sides and angles on each shape are marked using the same colours and symbols.
[11915|p371] [Image]
[11916|p371] [Image]
[11917|p371] [Image]
[11918|p371] [Image]
[11919|p371] When you make a congruency statement, you name the shape so that corresponding vertices are in the same order.
[11920|p371] For the shapes above:
[11921|p371] ABCD is congruent to EFGH
[11922|p371] MNOP is congruent to RQTS.

[11976|p374|T1] SUMMARY
[11977|p374|T1] Do you know ...?
[11978|p374] The longest side of a right-angled triangle is called the hypotenuse.
[11979|p374] The square of the hypotenuse is equal to the sum of the squares of the two shorter sides of the triangle.
[11980|p374] Similar shapes have equal corresponding angles and the ratios of corresponding sides are equal.
[11981|p374] If shapes are similar and the lengths of one shape are multiplied by a scale factor of [EQ] :
[11982|p374] then the areas are multiplied by a scale factor of [EQ]
[11983|p374] and the volumes are multiplied by a scale factor of [EQ]
[11984|p374] Congruent shapes are exactly equal to each other.
[11985|p374|T1] Are you able to...?
[11986|p374] Use Pythagoras' theorem to find an unknown side of a right-angled triangle.
[11987|p374] use Pythagoras' theorem to solve real-life problems
[11988|p374] decide whether or not two objects are mathematically similar
[11989|p374] Use the fact that two objects are similar to calculate:
[11990|p374] unknown lengths
[11991|p374] areas or volumes
[11992|p374] recognise whether two shapes are congruent or not.

[12047|p378] You can present statistical information in many different ways. If you choose a more complicated method, you increase the chance that another person will misinterpret the information. In the image on ...
[12048|p378] You do not always need to draw a diagram. Sometimes you present the information using a numerical summary. In this chapter you will explore different ways to present and compare data. Before you start...

[12064|p378|T1] 12.1 Different types of average
[12065|p378] The shoe sizes of 19 students in a class are:
[12066|p378] 47667486811686356764
[12067|p378] How would you describe the shape sizes in this class?
[12068|p378] Sometimes you might want to represent a set of data with a single value. When you do this, you use an average value.
[12069|p378] There are three main types of average used in statistics and you can use each of them to describe the shoe sizes for the class.
[12070|p378] The is the value that occurs the most often. If you count how times each shoe size occurs in the class, you will find that the most common (most frequent) size is six, so in this example the mode is 6...
[12071|p378|T1] KEY WORDS
[12072|p378] grouped data
[12073|p378] interquartile range
[12074|p378] lowerquartile
[12075|p378] 作
[12076|p378] median
[12077|p378] modal class
[12078|p378] mode
[12079|p378] range
[12080|p378] upper quartile
[12081|p378|T1] T
[12082|p378] There is more than one "average", so you should never refer to the average.
[12083|p378] Always specify which average you are talking about: the mean, median or mode.
[12084|p379] Another average is the mean. This is the value you get when you add up all the shoe sizes and divide your answer by the number of students:
[12085|p379] [Equation]
[12086|p379] The mean value tells you that the shoe sizes appear to be distributed in some way around the value 6.05. It also gives you a good impression of the general 'size' of the data. Notice that the value of...
[12087|p379] The third average is called the median. The median is the middle value when data is arranged in ascending order. So, putting the shoe sizes in order from smallest to largest gives:
[12088|p379] [Table Row] <tr><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>6</td><td>6</td><td>6</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>7</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>8</td><td>8</td><td>11</td></t...
[12089|p379] To find the middle number you can think of the first and last values as one pair and cross them off. Then continue crossing off pairs until you are left with a single value in the middle.
[12090|p379] [Table Row] <tr><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>...
[12091|p379] For these shoe sizes, the median value is 6.
[12092|p379] Crossing off numbers from each end can be difficult if you have a lot of data. If you count from the left, you find that the median is the 10th value. Adding one to the number of students and dividing...
[12093|p379] But what if there had been 20 students in the class? For example, if there is an extra student with a shoe size of 11. Crossing off pairs gives this result:
[12094|p379] [Table Row] <tr><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td>...
[12095|p379] You are left with a middle pair rather than a single value. If this happens then you need to find the mean of this middle pair: [EQ] .
[12096|p379] Notice that the position of the first value in this middle pair is [EQ] . If you have an even number of data values, dividing the number by two will always give you the first value of the middle pair.
[12097|p379] In summary:
[12098|p379] Mode The value that appears in the list more than any other. There can be more than one mode but if there are no values that occur more often than any other then there is no mode.
[12099|p379] Mean The value when you calculate total of all data number of values The mean may not be one of the actual data values.
[12100|p379] Median 1 Arrange the data into ascending numerical order.
[12101|p379] 2 If the number of data is [EQ] and [EQ] is odd, find [EQ] . This will give you the position of the median.
[12102|p379] 3 If [EQ] is even, then calculate [EQ] . This will give you the position of the first of the middle pair. Find the mean of this pair.
[12103|p379|T1] TIP
[12104|p379] If you take the mean of [EQ] items and multiply it by [EQ] , you get the total of all [EQ] values.
[12105|p379] The mean is sometimes referred to as a measure of 'central tendency' of the data.
[12106|p380|T1] LINK
[12107|p380] Many people use averages in their work. Geographers use them to summarise numerical results for large populations, so they don't have to show every piece of numerical data they collect.
[12108|p380] Medical scientists use means and medians to test whether or not a treatment makes a significant difference to the recovery of patients. They compare the average values for patients being tested with t...
[12109|p380] Estate agents often list the median value of property sales in an area because the median price is not influenced by extreme values.
[12110|p380] [Image]
[12111|p380|T1] Dealing with extreme values
[12112|p380] Sometimes your set of data may contain values that are extreme in some way. For example, if you measure the speeds of cars passing a certain point you may find that some cars are moving unusually slow...
[12113|p380] Imagine these are the speeds (in kilometres per hour) of six cars passing a certain point in a five-minute period:
[12114|p380] 67.2 58.3 128.9 65.0 49.0 55.7
[12115|p380] The value [EQ] seems much faster than any other car. How does this extreme value affect your averages?
[12116|p380] You can check yourself that the mean of the above data including the extreme value is [EQ]
[12117|p380] This average is larger than all but one of the values and is not representative of the data. This means that in this situation the mean might be a poor choice of average. If you discover that the high...
[12118|p380] If the extreme value is genuine and you cannot exclude it, then using the median is a better way to represent the main body of data. Start by writing the data in ascending order:
[12119|p380] 49.0 55.7 58.3 65.0 67.2 128.9
[12120|p380] The median is the mean of 58.3 and 65.0. This is 61.7. Notice that if you remove the highest value, the median reduces to 58.3, so this doesn't change things very much.
[12121|p380] There is no mode for these data.
[12122|p380|T1] TIP
[12123|p380] The number of data values is even, so the median is the mean of the 3rd and 4th data points.
[12124|p381|T1] LINK
[12125|p381] Most numerical quantities in physics are predictable, at least approximately, by using mathematical formulae to describe the situation. In these situations, extreme measurements that don't appear to f...

[12171|p382|T1] 12.2 Making comparisons using averages and ranges
[12172|p382] Now you know how to find an average value to represent your data, you can compare two or more sets of data. However, just comparing the averages can sometimes be misleading.
[12173|p382] It can also be helpful to know how consistent the data is. You do this by thinking about how scattered – or spread – the values are. A simple measure of spread is the range.
[12174|p382] Range = largest value - smallest value
[12175|p382|T1] TIP
[12176|p382] Think about whether the player will choose an average that helps them claim they are a good player or an average that is a fair representation of their scores.
[12177|p383] The larger the range, the more spread out the data is and the less consistent the values are with one another.

[12196|p383|T1] Units for averages
[12197|p383] You need to use the same units when comparing averages or ranges. There is no point in comparing one average or range measured in kilometres with another average or range measured in metres.

[12263|p386|T1] 12.3 Calculating averages and ranges for frequency data
[12264|p386] So far, you have calculated averages for quite small sets of data. When you have larger sets with more than 20 pieces of data, it is better to collect the data with the same value together and record ...
[12265|p386|T1] TIP
[12266|p386] Sometimes a frequency distribution table is simply called a frequency distribution.
[12267|p387|T1] Data shown in a frequency distribution table
[12268|p387] If you throw a single dice 100 times, each of the six numbers will appear several times. You can record the number of times each number appears in a table:
[12269|p387] [Table Row] <tr><td>Number showing on the upper face</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td></tr>
[12270|p387] [Table Row] <tr><td>Frequency</td><td>16</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>17</td><td>19</td><td>21</td></tr>
[12271|p387|T1] Mean
[12272|p387] To calculate the mean, you first need to find the total of all 100 throws. There are sixteen 1s, which give a subtotal of [EQ] , thirteen 2s giving a subtotal of [EQ] and so on. You can extend your ta...
[12273|p387] [Table Row] <tr><td>Number showing on the upper face</td><td>Frequency</td><td>Frequency x number on the upper face</td></tr>
[12274|p387] [Table Row] <tr><td>1</td><td>16</td><td>1 x 16 = 16</td></tr>
[12275|p387] [Table Row] <tr><td>2</td><td>13</td><td>2 x 13 = 26</td></tr>
[12276|p387] [Table Row] <tr><td>3</td><td>14</td><td>3 x 14 = 42</td></tr>
[12277|p387] [Table Row] <tr><td>4</td><td>17</td><td>17 x 4 = 68</td></tr>
[12278|p387] [Table Row] <tr><td>5</td><td>19</td><td>19 x 5 = 95</td></tr>
[12279|p387] [Table Row] <tr><td>6</td><td>21</td><td>21 x 6 = 126</td></tr>
[12280|p387] The total of all 100 throws is the sum of all values in this third column:
[12281|p387] [Equation]
[12282|p387] So the mean score per throw [EQ] .
[12283|p387|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[12284|p387] In Chapter 8 you studied the idea of 'experimental probability'. Notice that you also recorded data in frequency tables, so that you could estimate probabilities of different events.
[12285|p387|T1] Median
[12286|p387] There are 100 throws, which is an even number, so the median will be the mean of a middle pair. The first of this middle pair is in position [EQ] .
[12287|p387] The values are all in order in the table. The first 16 are 1s, the next 13 are 2s and so on. If you add the first three frequencies, you get [EQ] . This means that the first 43 values are 1, 2 or 3. T...
[12288|p387] [Image]
[12289|p387|T1] TIP
[12290|p387] You can add columns to a table given to you to help organise your calculations. In this case you need to swap the rows and columns to make it easier to use.
[12291|p388|T1] Mode
[12292|p388] For the mode you simply need to find the value that has the highest frequency. The number 6 occurs most often (21 times), so 6 is the mode.
[12293|p388|T1] Range
[12294|p388] The highest and lowest values are known, so the range is [EQ]
[12295|p388|T1] Data organised into a stem-and-leaf diagram
[12296|p388] You can determine averages and the range from stem-and-leaf diagrams.
[12297|p388|T1] Mean
[12298|p388] A stem-and-leaf diagram shows all the data values, so you find the mean by adding all the values and dividing them by the number of values in the same way you find the mean of any data set.
[12299|p388|T1] Median
[12300|p388] You can use an ordered stem-and-leaf diagram to find the median. In an ordered stem-and-leaf diagram the leaves for each stem are arranged in order from smallest to greatest.
[12301|p388|T1] Mode
[12302|p388] An ordered stem-and-leaf diagram allows you see which values are repeated in each row. You can compare these to find the mode.
[12303|p388|T1] Range
[12304|p388] You can use the first and last value in an ordered stem-and-leaf diagram to find the range.
[12305|p388] [Table Row] <tr><td>Stem</td><td>Leaf</td><td>Key</td></tr>
[12306|p388] [Table Row] <tr><td>0</td><td>2 5 5 6 6 6 6</td><td rowspan="3">0 | 2 represents 2 customers</td></tr>
[12307|p388] [Table Row] <tr><td>1</td><td>1 3 3 5 5 6 7 7</td></tr>
[12308|p388] [Table Row] <tr><td>2</td><td>1</td></tr>
[12309|p388|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[12310|p388] You learnt about stem-and-leaf diagrams in Chapter 4.

[12316|p389] [Equation]

[12318|p389] Row 1: [EQ] Row 2: [EQ] Row 3: 21 [EQ] customers in total

[12320|p389|T1] In summary:
[12321|p389] Mode The value that has the highest frequency is the mode. If more than one value has the same highest frequency, then there is no single mode.
[12322|p389] Mean total of all data sum of frequency [EQ] value number of values total frequency
[12323|p389] (Remember to extend the table so that you can fill in a column for calculating frequency [EQ] value in each case.)
[12324|p389] - Median - If the number of data values is [EQ] and [EQ] is odd, then [EQ] gives the position of the median. Add the frequencies until you reach the median position. The value in this position is the ...
[12325|p389] If [EQ] is even, then [EQ] gives the position of the first of the middle pair. Add the frequencies until you reach this position and then find the mean of the pair.

[12384|p391|T1] 12.4 Estimating the mean and finding the modal class for grouped data
[12385|p391|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[12386|p391] You learnt about discrete, continuous and grouped data in Chapter 4 and you used inequality notation to define the groups. You may find it helpful to review that work before continuing.
[12387|p391|T1] LINK
[12388|p391] Real-world data can involve thousands of data points, so it is clear that grouping data, rather than recording a list of exact values is sensible.
[12389|p391] When a government carries out a national census to collect numerical data about the population, the questions asked often involve ranges of ages, salaries and so on. It is not practical to record the ...
[12390|p391] When data is grouped in a frequency table you do not have the actual data points, so you have to work with the classes to estimate the mean and mode.
[12391|p391] The following worked example shows how a grouped frequency table is used to find the estimated mean and the modal class
[12392|p392|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 4
[12393|p392] The heights of 100 children were measured in cm and the results recorded in a table.
[12394|p392] [Table Row] <tr><td>Height in cm (h)</td><td>Frequency (f)</td></tr>
[12395|p392] [Table Row] <tr><td>120 &lt; h ≤ 130</td><td>12</td></tr>
[12396|p392] [Table Row] <tr><td>130 &lt; h ≤ 140</td><td>16</td></tr>
[12397|p392] [Table Row] <tr><td>140 &lt; h ≤ 150</td><td>38</td></tr>
[12398|p392] [Table Row] <tr><td>150 &lt; h ≤ 160</td><td>24</td></tr>
[12399|p392] [Table Row] <tr><td>160 &lt; h ≤ 170</td><td>10</td></tr>
[12400|p392] Find:
[12401|p392] a an estimate for the mean height of the children
[12402|p392] b the modal class.
[12403|p392|T1] Answers
[12404|p392] a You don't know any of the children's heights exactly, so you use the midpoint of each class as the best estimate of the height of each child in that class. For example, the 12 children in the [EQ] c...
[12405|p392] Halfway between [EQ] and [EQ] is [EQ] .
[12406|p392] A good estimate of the total height of the 12 children in this class is [EQ] (frequency [EQ] midpoint).
[12407|p392] Extend your table to include midpoints and totals for each class:
[12408|p392] [Table Row] <tr><td>Height in cm (h)</td><td>Frequency (f)</td><td>Midpoint (x)</td><td>Frequency x midpoint (fx)</td></tr>
[12409|p392] [Table Row] <tr><td>120 &lt; h ≤ 130</td><td>12</td><td>125</td><td>12 × 125 = 1500</td></tr>
[12410|p392] [Table Row] <tr><td>130 &lt; h ≤ 140</td><td>16</td><td>135</td><td>16 × 135 = 2160</td></tr>
[12411|p392] [Table Row] <tr><td>140 &lt; h ≤ 150</td><td>38</td><td>145</td><td>38 × 145 = 5510</td></tr>
[12412|p392] [Table Row] <tr><td>150 &lt; h ≤ 160</td><td>24</td><td>155</td><td>24 × 155 = 3720</td></tr>
[12413|p392] [Table Row] <tr><td>160 &lt; h ≤ 170</td><td>10</td><td>165</td><td>10 × 165 = 1650</td></tr>
[12414|p392] An estimate for the mean height of the children is:
[12415|p392] [Equation]
[12416|p392] b The class with the highest frequency is the modal class. In this case it is the class: [EQ] .
[12417|p392|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[12418|p392] Histograms provide a clear way of representing grouped data in graphical form. You will study these in Chapter 20.
[12419|p392|T1] TIP
[12420|p392] If a question asks you to explain why your calculations only give an estimate, remember that you don't have the exact data, only frequencies and classes.

[12453|p393|T1] TIP
[12454|p393] The symbol [EQ] is the Greek letter capital 'sigma'. It is used to mean 'sum'. So, [EQ] simply means 'the sum of all the frequencies'.
